to the Welfare of the State.
CALL, HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO, Jan. 4.â Henry T. Gage is
now the Governor of Californiaâ not
the Governor of Republicans alone, but
the Governor of all the people. He was
inaugurated with the pomp and cere
mony that is a necessary adjunct of
such an occasion. It is creditable t" thÂ»-
management of the inaugural commit
tee of the Senate and Assembly that
everything passed off with celerity. The
military was out in sufficient force to
indicate that soldiers can be had in
time of peace as well as in war. The
field and staff officers of the National
Guard in their glittering uniforms gave
color to the procession and the State
officers lent dignity to the occasion.
The weather was propitious and the in
c>rning Governor was got. J-natuved
and genial.
About 11 o'clock this morning Gov
ernor Gage drove to the capital and

ager?. Presently he was surrounded

Senator Dickinson in the full uniform

1,. Peeler was hardly less conspicuous

Seated in comfortable and clean car

, was drawn by six spirited white horses.
i The Lieutenant Governor, Jacob H.
Neff, the outgoing Lieutenant Gover
nor. William T. Jeter, and Howard

cupied another carriage.
Before the procession of State and

ladies escorted by Colonel Burgin were

eral Barrett, Adjutant General Peeler

cers.
Shortly after the ladies took their
leave General Barrett marshaled the

rected the procession to move. The
short journey to the Capitol was made
Without special incident. The trans
portation facilities were ample and no
one entitled by courtesy to ride was

along X and Tenth streets to L and
along L to the north entrance to the

large throngs of the resident popula
tion wended their way to the CapitoL
The galleries of the Assembly chamber
were quickly tilled. The corridors were

culty that the places reserved for Sena
tors and State officers could be pro
tected from tht? rush of people to gain

In due form the Joint convention was
organized. Lieutenant Governor Jeter
directed Frank Brandon, secretary of

was apprised that the big- lights were

Almighty God. we acknowledge thy
hand in all the affairs of human govern

THE INAUGURAL ADDRESS.
Gentlemen of the Senate and Assembly
of the State of California and fellow citi
zens:
To faithfully fulfill the solemn duties
imposed upon me by the official oath Just
administered is my unalterable purpose.
Individuals arc. at best, but weak in
struments to guide- the destiny of a State.
Conscious of this truth, and" being fully
aware of the grave responsibilities inci
dent to this sacred trust of office now
Imposed upon me, in order that I may
be rightly guided in my public duties. I
humbly repose my faith in that Omnis
cient Power which alike directs the
course of nations as individuals.

foreign war. Springing from the highest

The members of the Legislature and
spectators listened with marked atten
tion to the delivery of the address. Ap
plause was evoked when the sentiment
in favor of retaining the Philippines
whs uttered. The allusion to the amount
charged by the newspapers for publish
ing constitutional amendments created
a ripple of merriment and caused slight
applause. Perhaps neither the audi
ence nor the Governor understood that
the amendments were published by offi
cial direction and that the charge ren
dered by the newspapers was the regu
lar rate for the space occupied and is
the same as is paid by private indi
viduals.
Immediately after the minutes of the
session were approved the joint conven
tion dissolved. Thereupon Governor
Budd escorted his successor to the ex
ecutive office and there surrendered
everything in sight to Mr. Gage. Pretty
soon Governor Budd's staff officers filed
in to pay their respects to their com
mander-in-chief, and they were also
sin rendered to Governor Gage. As the
new Governor reviewed in his glances
the fine figures of Colonel Joe Howell
and genial Colonel Beck and the rest of
the heroic band his eye sparkled with
delight. He must have said to himself,
as he looked at them: "This is worth
all the struggle for the office." Later
on members of the Legislature, State
officers and ladies and gentlemen resid
ing in Sacramento came forward to
greet the newly inaugurated executive.
The Democrats of the Legislature did
a graceful tiling. They called in a
body to pay their respects to Governor
Gage and give a parting salute to Gov
ernor Budd. The ex-Governor, accom
panied by his mother and wife, left
Sacramento on the 5 o'clock train for
Stockton. The Democratic legislators
went tn the railroad station and there
bade him good-by, with many wishes
for his future prosperity and happi
ness.

TELEPHONE COMPANY
INFLUENCE FOR BURNS
CAT.T, HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO. Jan. 4. âThe Pacific Bell Tele
phone Company is the latest corporation
in come out in the open In its support <>f
Dan Burns for Senator, and to-day it was

UNCLE BILLY BOWERS
SIZES THE SITUATION
CALL, HEADQUARTERS. SACRA
MENTO. Jan. A. â Oim: of the. old-time
politicians of California is . ex-Congress
man W. W. Bowers of San Diego. Down
in the Seventh Congressional .District,
which he for six years represented in the
halls of the National Legislature, the
men and women refer to him as Uncle
"Billy" Bowers.
There is one quality Uncle Billy has. He
is very plain spoken. He has partici
pated in a number of Senatorial fights in
California in times prone by. for he has on
several occasions, going as far hack as
1876 been a member both of the Senate
and of the Assembly. Since Monday the
ex-Congressman has been here watching
the Senatorial fight and as a loyal San
Diegan doing what he can to assist in
booming Mr. Grant. Asked to: give his
views on the situation as he looked upon
it to-night. Uncle Billy in characteristic
language said:
"Well, now, I'll tell you. There is no
man got this light won now. It's the fun
niest thing I ever saw. There are factors
at work in this bout that I never saw he
fore in any Senatorial fight. The one
thing that complicates more than ' any
thing else is the patronage of the State
administration. That's what mixes the
thing up so that men don't know where
they stand. I want to tell you that if. you
will take the patronage of the State ad
ministration out Of this battle U. S. Grant
will get the caucus nomination beyond
the question of a doubt."

THE CHARTER WILL BE RATIFIED.
CALL HEADQUARTERS.. SACRAMENTO. Jan. 4.â There is no longer any
doubt whatever that the new charter adopted by the people of San Francisco will be ratified
by the Legislature. Some weeks ago The Call made a poll of the Legislature by telegraph,
and. while a majority of the members of both houses declared in favor of its ratification, there
were a number who expressed a desire to more thoroughly familiarize themselves with the
instrument before committing themselves for or against it.
During the present week The Call's staff of correspondents has made a systematic and
intelligent canvass of the Legislature, with the result that it is enabled to announce to the peo
ple of San Francisco that there will be practically no opposition to the charter in either House.
And there will be little if any delay in its ratification. It will be presented to the Senate and
Assembly to be acted upon as soon as the Legislature is in smooth working order, and there
is no likelihood that any effort will be made to block its course.

DECLINE THIS
EMPTY HONOR
No CF)eap Brevet Ranl^ for H im
When He Feels That He Has
Won a Medal.
NEW YORK, Jan. 4.â The friends of Governor Roosevelt in this city
were not pleased when they read in ihe Herald to-day that the War De
partment had recommended to the President that the colonel of the
Rough Riders he made a brevet brigadier general and that the President
would act favoring the recommendation.
If Colonel Roosevelt is made a brevet brigadier general he will prob
ably lifeline the honor, and there is reason to believe that the head of
the War Department knows that Colonel Roosevelt will decline.
Colonel Roosevelt's friends say that what he wants is the medal of
honor to which he is entitled, and which the War Department is with
holding because of th^ colonel's famous "round robin." They also say
that th^ manner in which the brevet rank is to be given, in connection
with other volunteer colonels who were not under fire at all, is humili
ating.
The Btory as was told to-night is that Colonel Roosevelt was recom
mended for a medal of honor by General Shatter, General Wheeler and
General Wood. Thus the corps commander, division commander and
brigade commander joined In the request. No doubt, it was said, the
medal of honor would have been given but for the fact that Colonel
Roosevelt's desire to get hia troops away from Santiago when they
were likely to die of yellow fever stirred up the War Department.
Tlie news from Washington is taken by Colonel Roosevelt's friends
to mean that the recommendations of Generals Shafter, Wheeler and
W 1 are to be Ignored and that Colonel Roosevelt is nut to have his
m< lal of honor, but an empty title instead, the same as Colonel H. T.
P.ai-her of the First New York, who did not fight at Honolulu, and Col
on. '1 .1. B. Castleman of the First Kentucky, who was in Porto Rico at
one time under General Schwan, but was not even in the Tight which
Schwan had at Mayaguez.
FRENCH SHORE OF
NEWFOUNDLAND
Fisheries May Yet Lead
to Trouble.
â 
LAND OF LITTLE VALUE
ENGLISH STATESMEN IN UGLY
HUMOR.
There May Be Cause for War in This
Between the French
and the Brit
ish.
Special ("able to The Call and the New York
Herald Copyrighted. 1898, by James Gor
don Bennett. _
PARIS, Jan. 4.â There seems to be a
growing conviction here that the New
foundland question will furnish the
next subject for discussionâ some even
go bo far as to say for disputeâbe
tween England and France. The mat
ter has been referred to many times
lately. Mr. McGrath, editor of the St.
John Evening Herald, has contributed
an article to the current Contemporary
l;. lew wherein the English view of the
case is given and reprisals are
threatened if satisfaction be not ob
tained.
"Satisfaction" appears to mean in the
nt case that the French should
withdraw from the stand they have
taken on their incontestable legal rights
guaranteed them by the treaty of
Utrecht. These rights have no longer
any raison d'etre, according to the
English. Mr. Chamberlain has pointed
out that at the time the treaty was
concluded the fisheries were uf great
value; now they have become compara
tively i'.isigniii( ant.
Hear Admiral Reveillere, in a letter
written to-day to Le Matin, says the
French Bhore is of no interest to our
navy. He affirms that the question of
the French shore- in Newfoundland is
us only because it has been ab
surdly exaggerated by professional
patriots.
"The English have never disputed the
rights of France," he writes. "They
only say nothing can be nv>re true than
that our ] ;; the French shore
Is a source "f perpetual danger from
conflicts which have only been averted
by extreme good will on the part of
both governments.
"It would certainly be of benefit to
both nations," concludes the admiral.
"if our rights could be exchanged tor
an equivalent compensation. I posi
tively affirm, certain that I shall not
be contradicted by any officers Who
know the Newfoundland station, that
the French shore is of no interest to
our navy."
Marc Landy, in Figaro, admits that
tli" entire territory in dispute is not
worth the blood of a single French
Bailor. "We should be well advised In
accepting compensation in exchange for
this stretch of coast," is his conclusion.
The question is will both parties ap
proach the subject in this conciliatory
spirit.

CLAMORING TO
LAND AT HAWAII
Chinese Do Not Relish
Annexation.
OUR LAWS NOT WELCOME
MONGOLS WHO HAVE RE
TURNED MAKE A CONTEST.
The Supreme Court of the Islands De
cided in Their Favor, and an
Appeal Has Been Taken
to Washington.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
Call Office, Riggs House,
Washington, Jan. 4.
Assistant Secretary HoweU of the
Treasury Department has under con
sideration the cases of a number of
Chinese seeking; readmiasion to Hawaii,
and has submitted the questions in
volved to Attorney General Griggs. At
different periods before the annexation
of Hawaii to this country many Chinese
left Hawaii with the intention of re
turning after going to China on visits
or engaging in business elsewhere. They
compiled with the Hawaiian laws en
titling them to return.
When they learned of the annexation
of the islands and the establishment of
United States laws over Hawaii many
<if them hastened back to the islands.
Inspector Brown of the United States
Gi .rnment denied them admission on
the ground that under the law.s of the
United Stuns they were not entitled to
come back. The case was carried be
fore Justice Judd of the Hawaiian Su
preme Court. He decided that the
Chinese ought to be admitted.
Brown has taken an appeal to the
Treasury Department, and the case will
be given careful consideration. Under
the laws of this country a Chinese go
ing away must secure from the Collector
of the Port from which he sails a cer
tificate that he has a family or prop
erty here and is entitled to return with
in a year.
The attorneys for the Chinese in Ha
waii maintain that it is unfair to en
force the laws of the United States
upon people who went out of Hawaii
believing that tiny could return under
the laws which then existed. It is
thought likely that the Attorney Gen
eral will uphold the decision of the Ha
waiian .lu.lp.- and admit the Chinese.
Nearly 800 Chinese will be debarred un
less the decision holds.

The Water Was Boiling.
T. J. Roberts has sued W. R. Hearst
to recover $5000 damages for personal in
juries. Plaintiff alleges that on January
6, 1897, while he was employed in the en
gine room of the Examiner his foot
slipped and plunged into an uncovered
excavation which was filled with boiling
water. He avers that his foot and leg
were badly injured: hence the suit.

Cipher Dispatches Received From the
Philippines Have Created the
Wildest Consternation in
Administration Circles.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WASHINGTON, Jan. 4.â
Administration circles were
thrown into consternation by the
receipt of a dispatch from Gen
eral Otis to-night giving the de
tails of a plot among the Fili
pinos, under the leadership of
Aguinaldo, to resist American
control in the Philippine Islands
by force of arms if necessary.
Owing to the reticence of the
â¢als little beyond that hinted
at above can be learned regard
lie contents of Otis' dispatch.
That it was deemed of great im
portance is evidenced by the fact
that upon its receipt at the War
Department it. was ordered im
mediately transcribed from the
ripher and taken at once to the
White Mouse for submission to
i he President by General Corbin.
At the War Department it was
ordered asserted that Otis had
Aguinaldo under surveillance,
but at what point the dispatch
didn't set forth. It was given as
an opinion, however, that the in
surgent leader is at Malolos,
where he had called the insurgent
Congress to meet on Thursday,
which is to-day in that longitude.
Why, if Aguinaldo's influence
is feared or if he is believed to be
inciting the natives to insurrec
tion, he is not under arrest, is not
explained. In fact, it is believed
by army officers that Aguinaldo
has been arrested and that the
revolution or insurrection against
American authority is really on,
and that this portion of Otis' dis
patch is being withheld from the
public.
There is much gossip in mili
tary circles regarding the ability
of Otis and Miller to hold their
own against the insurgents if in
reality an uprising has occurred.
In a dispatch last night Otis ex
pressed the utmost confidence in
being able to cope with Agui
naldo's army should a conflict oc
cur and said his force was large
enough to meet any emergency.
His sending the First Califor
nia Regiment to reinforce Miller
nt Iloilo is taken here as proof
that reports of the size of the Fil
ipino army on the island of Lu
zon have been exaggerated.
General Otis has now at Manila
19,000 men. and it is regarded as
certain that he would not have
reduced his force if Aguinaldo's
army had been larger than his.
The insurgents suffered great
ly in effective strength through
the capture by Dewey of the
steamer Abbey, with 400,000
stands of arms and ammunition
for Aguinaldo.
It is not denied here that Agui
naldo has plenty of men, but the
War Department is counting as
effective, in the event of a clash,
only those Who carry rifles.
When a military official was
asked to-night â  how many men
Aguinaldo had in the vicinity of
Manila he said:
â¢ "It is pretty hard to tell where
the army leaves off and the mob
begins."
If any notice was received from
Otis up to midnight that he had
issued his proclamation to the
Filipinos it was not made public,
and at the War Department,
when told that The Call had a
cable to the effect that the proc
lamation had been madej it was
THE FILIPINOS MUST
LAY DOWN THEIR ARMS.
MAXILA, Jan. 4.â A1l is quiet here. The American troops which arrived
at lloilo have not landed. They are awaiting instructions. The native gov
ernment is firmly established there.
The merchants all desire that the Americans shall plant a firm govern
â  ment in the islands.
A proclamation, based on the cabled instructions by the President, was
Issued by General Otis at 5 o'clock this evening. The proclamation says it is
' the intention of the United States to draw from the Filipinos as much of the
military force of the islands as possible and to seek the establishment of a
most liberal government, in which the Filipinos will have as full representa^
â¢ tion as maintenance of law and order will permit.
No mention is made of the kind of government to be established, or of
' the number of representatives the Filipinos will be allowed to have, two points
" on which the natives are extremely anxious for information. The proclama
. tion is also indefinite on other points.
A demand is made that the Filipinos lay down their arms, but no date is
â¢ set for them to do so.

it was considered doubtful, as it
was not believed Otits would
take such an important step with
out informing the home Govern
ment of it.
Otis' plan, as outlined in dis
patches received yesterday, was
to publish the proclamation in
pamphlet form and distribute it
at all important places, including
Malolos, where the delegates to
the Filipino Congress were to
gather, in order that the latter
might be fully informed of the in
tentions of the American Gov-'
ernment before taking any action
in the matter of sanctioning
Aguinaldo in further opposition
to American control.
The Navy Department re
ceived dispatches from Dewey to
night, but according to the story
given to the press these related
only to sending war vessels to
the ports of the Philippines to an
nounce the assertion of American
control.
THE CALIFORNIA BOYS
NOT HOMEWARD BOUND
Call Office, Rlggs House.
Washington, D. C. Jan. 4.
When the formal order was to-day
issued mustering out the Eighth Cali
fornia Regiment and companies B and
C of the California Heavy Artillery the
Adjutant General said incidentally th.it
nothing- definite could be saM t s to the
First California Infantry, now at M.i
nila or Iloilo. The regulars are ex
pected to leave New York in a week or
ten days. It was expected that several
of the regiments of volunteers at Ma
nila would be relieved upon their ar
rival, and that the First California
would be among these. But the
trouble with the insurgents, especially
at Iloilo, may change the situation.
The department hopes that these
troubles will be speedily settled, but
ti;- officials have no Information upon
which to predicate an opinion of the
real situation there. The officials are
anxiously awaiting advices and honing
that those advices will show a chance
for the better.
It is the general opinion, however,
that a conflict cannot be avoided if the
United States forces attempt to land
at Iloilo. Of course a landing could
have been effected by force at any time
the forts were shelled, the city re
duced and the insurgents driven out.
Such a course, however, is just what
this Government is most desirous of
avoiding, and consequently General
Miller has used only conciliatory meas
ures in dealing with the natives.
In the meantime, however. Aguinaldo
has been scouring the adjoining islands
fnr native reinforcements, which have
poured Into Iloilo every day. greatly
Increasing the army there. It is thought
now that should General Miller attempt
to land in spite of the prohibition of
the native chiefs a bloody battle might
ensue.
The last dispatch received at the War
Department from General Otis an
nounced that he would send the First
California Regiment to reinforce Gen
eral Miller. The published dispatches
from Manila this morning announced
that this had been done. The regiment
was embarked on the transports Cam
pania and Maritima and convoyed to
Iloilo by the gunboat Concord.
This action of General Otis was some
what of a surprise to department offi
cials, as he had been instructed to do
nothing which would weaken his force
at Manila. He was not absolutely
bound by those instructions, however,
and the fact that he has sent reinforce
ments to General Miller is thought to
indicate that he regards the situation
at Qoilo as Berious. General Miller has
now something over : j ,000 men under
him. The cruiser Baltimore is the flag
ship of the small naval detachment
there, which is commanded by Captain
D y er - * i
The Insurgent forces numbered only
about 1500 when the city was turned
over to them by the Spaniards, but they
have been greatly augmented since. It
is feared that Aguinaldo is organizing
his forces t<> resist Americans wherever
resistance is possible, and a dispatch
announcing that a conflict had taken
place would cause little surprise at any
time.
THE SPANIARDS DENY
CHARGE OF BAD FAITH
MADRID, Jan. 4.â The statement that
the Spaniards evacuated Iloilo in order
to render the situation difficult for the
Americans is positively denied. The
Government, immediately after the
treaty had br-.i signed, ordered the
Philippines evacuated as quickly as
possible, in order to avoid further
bloodshed and to conform to the ex
pressed wish of the Americans, who
were very exacting, especially in the
case of Cuba.
An interview with an ex-prefect of a
province of the Philippines is published
here, in the course of which he is quoted
as saying:
"President McKinley has shown com
plete ignorance of the situation in the
Philippines. When the Americans oc
cupied Manila there were only four in
surgent provinces. The Americans en
couraged the revolution and now they
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, JANUARY 5, 1899.
themselves are the victims of the sup
port which they gave the insurgents,
who have become masters of the entire
archipelago except Alindanao, where
the natives, who are Mussulmans, re

"Aguinaldo's unpopularity is due to

Loud's Latest Postal Bill.
"WASHINGTON, Jan. 4. â Chairman
Loud of the House Postoffice Committee
to-day submitted a favorable report on
his bill to amend the postal laws relating
to second class mall matter. A similar
bill passed the House in 1897, but failed in
the Senate. It provides that books or re
prints of books in serial form shall pay
one cent for two ounces instead of one
cent per pound; publications of institu
tions of learning are placed on the same
footing as other periodicals, instead of
being privileged. The report submitted
with the former bills is made to apply to
this one.

COST OF THE BIG
ARMY PROPOSED
Figures Submitted on
the Hull Bill.
NEARLY SIXTY MILLIONS MORE
HEADS OF DEPARTMENTS MAKE
ESTIMATES.
According to the Measure to Reor
ganize the Military Establish
ment, the Troops Would Cost
$82,052,865 Yearly.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WASHINGTON, Jan. 4.â The cost of
the proposed military establishment
under the Hull bill reported to the
House, as compared with the present
regular military establishment, not
counting the volunteer forces incident
to the war, is shown in letters sent by
Adjutant General Co-rbin to Chairman
Hull of the House Military Committee.
It gives the present cost of the regular
army at $24,443,480; cost under the pro
posed bill, $52,053,865; increase of cost,
$57,710,231.
The cost as applied to the three main
departments of the army is as follows:
Quartermaster's department, present
cost, $7,107,000; propped, $40,013,246.
Subsistence department, present cost,
$2,739,775; proposed cost. $10,148,478.
Pay department, present cost, $14,496,
--659; proposed cost, $31,891,943.
General Corbin incloses letters from
Quartermaster General Ludington,
Commissary General Eagan and Pay
master General Stanton showing the
details of the comparison. The main
items of increase in the quartermaster's
department are:
Transportation, from $2,300,000 to $17,
--700,000; clothing and equipment, from
$975,000 to $5,952,246; regular supplies,
from $1,800,000 to $7,200,000; barracks,
quarters and military posts, from
$1,170,000 to $4,000,000; hospitals, $90,000
to $360,000; incidental expenditures,
$600,000 to $2,400,000.
General Eagan gives the Increased
cost of each branch of the service, in
cluding active, sick and convalescent.
General Stanton says the pay esti
mate does not cover the 20 per cent to
enlisted men during time of war or the
proposed 25 per cent to those serving in
distant lands.

SOAPY SMITH'S
IMITATOR LYNCHED
AT EAGLE CITY
Jack Jolly, Sa!oon=Keeper and
Desperado, Is Given Short
Shrift by Enraged
Miners.
VANCOUVER, B. C, Jan. 4.â W. Devine of San Francisco, who
arrived here direct from Dawson to-night on the steamer Rosalie,
says that Jack Jolly, the wealthiest gambler and saloon-keeper in
the northern gold fields, was lynched on December 11 by a vigilance
committee of Eagle City, Alaska.
The story of Jolly's death is full of dramatic incidents. He at
tempted with a gang of reckless men to be the "Soapy" Smith of Eagle
City. As robbery after robbery occurred in his saloon sober-minded
miners warned him that he was going too far. He laughed at them,
and finally they formed themeslves into a vigilance committee. Jolly
was given forty-eight hours to leave town. He dared the vigilance
committee to drive him out and called his gang around him. Fifty
minors, fully armed, surrounded his hotel on December 9 and de
manded his surrender. Jolly's followers abandoned him, and after a
bloodless struggle the desperado was captured. He was taken to a
tree near his saloon and was given three minutes to say his prayers.
Curses were his answer.
The end came quickly. A noose was thrown around his neck and
while his thoroughly cowed gang watched from a distance he was
swung up, dying game to the last. According to Devine, Jolly has had
Eagle City in a state fo terror formonths. He defied the law and no
respectable man was safe in his saloon. Several Montana miners
commenced the agitation against him and the feeling of the better
element became so strong that it was known two months ago he
would either have to leave town or die. His tragic death caused a
sensation in Dawson, where he was reputed to be worth several hun
dred thousand dollars.
Jolly originally came from Nevada. He was a splendid specimen
of physical manhood and was a power among the reckless element
of the north on account of his extreme daring. Devine, with W. H.
"Walsh of Portland, left Dawson December 12, being the last to come
out. He made the record trip of the season, reaching Skaguay in
seventeen days.

CONCENTRATION OF
CUSTOMS MONEY
Excitement at Santiago
Uncalled For.
CUBANS ARE TO BE AIDED
COMMUNITIES CANNOT HAVE
ALL COLLECTED.
However, Proper Orders Have Been
Issued for the Belief of Dis
tress Existing in Various
Quarters.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WASHINGTON, Jan. 4.â Regarding
the sensational reports from Santiago
that the whole province has been in
flamed by the recent order of the War
Department "concentrating all customs
money in Havana," thus threatening a
stoppage of all public improvements
and threatening to throw thousands of
Cubans out of employment, it may be
authoritatively stated that any excite
ment existing in Santiago is due to a
misapprehension of the War Depart
ment's order. It seems to have been
taken for granted that the order con
templates sending all currency collected
for customs dues to Havana, and thus
cutting off the Governor of Santiago
province from the employment of local
labor. The fact is that the officers in
charge of customs at the various i><>rts
of entry are merely ordered to "make
weekly reports to the collector of cus
toms of the island at the chief port of
all transactions at the sub-port over
which he may have jurisdiction, with
copies of entries of merchandise duly
certified."
The customs from all the ports of the
island are to be used for the benefit of
the Cubans, not only in Havana and
Santiago, but elsewhere.
Expenditures will be divided as equit
ably as possible, so as to- relieve dis
tress existing in various quarters. It
would be just as equitable, it is argued,
for the port of New York to retain all
the customs funds collected there for
expenditure on the island of Manhattan
as it would be for Havana or Santiago
or any other town In Cuba to gobble all
of the customs receipts for expenditure
within its own borders.
So far from the War Department
contemplating any curtailment of the
relief work In Cuba an order already
has been issued to the Governor Gen
eral directing him to furnish work to
every able-bodied Cuban applying for
it at a fair rate of weekly wages, meas
ured by Cuban standards. The order
suggests that the best lines of employ
ment are in sanitary work, road im
provement and municipal work of va
rious sorts.
It is intended to offer a chance for
remunerative work to all who are able
to take advantage of it and to cut off
the gratuitous distribution of rations
among those natives able to work for
what they eat. The employment con
templated will not be offered, however,
to any resident of the island who re
tains his allegiance to Spain.
A Boy Who Was Too Smart.
There's a youngster on Joy street who
hears things, remembers them and util
izes them in his own way at home.
The other night there was company
for dinner and Jimmie startled every one
when he thus tried to relieve a. brief si
lence:
"Papa you smell like a Chinese laun
dryman.
"What do you mean, you impudent
boy?" blurted out the father, both embar
rassed and angry.
"Through your rose, of course," and
Jimmy could hear laughter after he had
gone to bed under imperative orders.
Several weeks ago there was a meet
ing of charitably Inclined ladies at the
house and Jimmie entered in the midst of
their deliberattions. After listening long
enough to get the thread of the discussion,
he took a hand.
"Mamma," Jimmie interrupted, in a sad
voice, "I know a man not three blocks
from here that hasn't given his wife a
thing to eat for more than a week."
The good women went into a perfect
spasm of indignation, some of them go
ing fo far as to declare that the monster
should be lynched. But the mother was
suspicious of the demure look on Jimmies
face. "Who is this cruel man, my son?"
"Mr. Loner."
"Mr. I. oner." gasped the excited ladles,
"Why the dear old man has no wife and
never had."

Was He BluffingP
At 4 o'clock one Friday afternoon not
long ago, as Mr. T. was busily engaged at
his desk in a downtown office, a man
walked in and inquired for Mr. 8., who
occupies a desk at the opposite side of the
same office. Mr. T. thought he recog
nized the man as an unwelcome caller.
When asked if Mr. B. was in, without
raising his eyes from the work before
him, Mr. T. answered, "No, he is not,"
whereupon the caller remarked that as ha
was not very busy he thought he would
sit down and wait.
At 5 o'clock he was still waiting.
At 5:30 still waiting.
A few minutes before 6 Mr. T. closed
his desk for the day and prepared to go
home. The caller ventured to ask if Mr.
B. was likely to return to his office again
that day. Mr. T. answered:
"No; he is in Buffalo, and will be back
next Tuesday morning."
The caller showed no anger. On tha
contrary i be smiled.
"Don't apologize," he said; "my. busi
ness was not important, and your â¢"office
has proved a pleasant lounging place.
Fact is," ho blandly added, "I suppose
I'm coming down with the smallpox, and
tho doctor told me I must stay indoors
and keep warm."
And now Mr. T. anxiously wonders
whether he was lying, or not.â Cleveland
Plain Dealer.
Cheap Cars for Smokers.
The new electric tram service which
has been established by the Liverpool
Corporation was opened the other day for
passenger traffic. The line, which is
equipped on the overhead trolley system,
is about three miles in length, running
from the center of the city to the Dingle,
one of the southern suburbs. The cars,
of German make, run in couplesâ one for
smokersâ and stop at stations about 200
yards apart, completing the journey in
twenty-five minutes. The fare is one
penny for smokers, and two-pence in tha
general cars. The traffic of the new line
was about treble that of the old horse car
scrvier, which was much more expensive.
The line has been constructed as an ex
periment to determine whether the over
head system whs suitable for the busy
streets in the center of the city before
establishing electric traction over tha
whole of the hundred miles of tram routes
within the city boundaries. â London
Telegraph.

GOVERNOR BUDD'S. MESSAGE.
GOVERNOR BUDD'S message is so long that
comparatively few people will read it. yet it ;
has sufficient strength to support its weight
and will well repay the attentive and careful study of
all who take an intelligent interest in public attairs.
Moreover, it is a message which can be read with
more than ordinary gratification, since it records no- â 
table reforms and improvements in the conduct of the ;
affairs of the commonwealth, and suggests others
which might be wisely adopted.
The salient- feature of the message is the showing
made ot increased economy in the State government.
To achieve this the Governor points out that his
vetoes for the sessions of 1895 and 1897. inclusive,
amounted to - ... and ot this sum he says over
lour millions is a saving to the people. The record is
one which the taxpayers as well as the Governor can
regard with no little satisfaction.
On the subject of taxation generally the mess -
has much to say, and a considerable space is given to
â¢suggestions in the direction of tax reform. Under
our present system the rate of the tax levy is in the
hands of the Board of Equalization rather than in that
of the Legislature. If the Equalizers place a high
valuation upon the property of the State the tax rate
required to raise p. given amount of revenue will be '
low in comparison with the rate required to raise an
equal sum if the valuation is lowered.
During the past four years the Equalizers have al
ternately lowered and raised the assessment, and the
Governor asserts that this year certain members of
the Board of Equalization "telegraphed to Boards of
Supervisors suggesting they ask for a reduction in j
the valuation in their counties, and many counties |
were reduced."' The evil resulting from such a condi
tion of affairs is stated as follows:
"A high tax rate is injurious to any State because â¢
people abroad, seeking investment, ask the rate, and ,
do not understand the causes that have led a Board of
Equalization to play battledore and shuttlecock with
State" Values. They do not understand the combinn
tions of Equalizers. They do understand, however, !
that'the constant shrinkage of values would ordinarily
indicate a retrogadir.g instead of a progressing State. ,
and that low tax rates destroy property as a basis for
loans "
The Call has pointed out the advantages which
would result from such a change in the laws as would
require Equalizers to be chosen by the people of the
State at large instead of by districts, and the facts sub
mitted by the Governor strengthen the argument for
the suggested reform. The message, however, advo
cates a wider innovation, and proposes a complete re
modeling of our tax system. Some striking instances
. are given to show the injustice of the present system
and the extentto which personal property escapes
taxation.
; . .Another important recommendation is the creation
c: -a" Central State Board, to consist of the Governor
â¢,and two State officers, to have general charge of mak
ing contracts for State buildings and to regulate sala
ries and number of employes, etc., in State institu
tions. Such a board would enab'e many- reforms to
be carried out which are now impossible because of
the lack of a: , authority to enforce them.
Hardly any department or branch of the State
government is overlooked in the message, and wher
ever evils are noted well considered reforms are sug
gested. It is a state paper which should be thor
oughly studied by. the legislators, for many of the re
commendations made ought to be carried out at this
session.
The present Board 'of Education has {he advan
tage of being displayed against the background of the
-rascality of the old set. If fairly honest, they will
shine as mode's of virtue by contrast, and if con
scientious and thorough they will be rated as but
little lower than the angels.
. There was no necessity for a Police Commissioner
â¢ to*' say he had not read the statement of the intended
] raid on the police force in the interest of Dan Burns.
He merely exposes himself to the danger of not be
; ing believed.

GOVERNOR GAGES BOORISHNESS.
HENRY T. GAGE is now Governor of Califor
nia. The position is one exalted in station.
The incumbent has power to promote good
government, to advance economical methods, arul in
great measure to check the spoilsman. He can dis
courage corruption, overthrow abuses. He can
bring to the executive chair dignity, and be an honor
to the place which is an honor to him.
But because such rich opportunities present them
selves there doc? not follow a certainty that they will
be grasped. No assurance exists that an individual
will live measurably near to the ideal. Already it is
to be observed with regret that Governor Gage is too
small to boar well the trust imposed. A large man
regards himself as chosen to be the servant
of the people A small man swells to absurd propor
5, and fondly imagines that he buiges impress
ively. Or he can listen to the voice of the scheming
politician and think it the mandate of the party or the
commonwealth. These are errors into which Gage
seem? already to have fallen. Unless he taice steps to
extricate himself at once he will become hopelessly
! n-.ireJ.

no good. They are doing harm to the party

ndicates the preference of the Presi
they are insulting the man whose commission
they bear. The President takes no part in the elec
tion of a Senator in California, and his own
appointees deserve rebuke for placing him in a false
; â  tion.
Because these office-holders have received appoint
ts and are drawing salaries is no reason why they
should attempt to control the Senatorial choice of the
and force it to do an unclean thing. There is
not a decent man in California that does not know
that the election of Burns will inflict an injury upon
the Republican party from which it will not recover
f r years. What right, then, have these men to strike

: party success by an act which makes such success for

Not only every self-respecting Republican who has

lican who has ambitions that can be realized only by
future party victories, should say to these Federal

ito the party and an insult to the State. They should

i -.vhich they are paid and which they neglect to engage
i in while cutting their party's throat
It is hinted that Burns has long tipped off races on

dered home, but dismissed from their offices or sur

r racing tips the Federal

is not dragged in the mire by such methods
These office-holders got enough when they got on
the payroll. Let them keep hands off the party
and attend to the duties for which they are paid.
A BOOM FOR GORMAN.
AS a result of the Wilson tariff and the Bryan
campaign for free silver the Democratic party
.en so weakened that when the next Con
â  eets there will be not a single straight Demo
crat in the Senate from the Northern States. Nor will
there be a solid Democratic representation from the
South, as the Republicans will have ten Senators from
tion. The conservative branch of the Govern
ment will be safely in the hands of Republicans, and
free trade and free silver are no longer issues of prac
tical politic?.
Under these circumstances it is natural the Demo
crat? should begin to look for a leader who is not
identified with either the free trade or the free silver
fiasco. Even among Democrats who believe in those
theories many are no longer in favor of making an
other fight for them. The Nashville American, for
example, says: "We are Democratic from headpiece
to footslug. but we believe we are sagacious enough
tn see a brick wall when it stands in our pathway."
and it adds: 'There is a chance for Democracy to
make a strong fight in 1900. but it is not by blindly
forcing leaders or issue? to the front that have been
rejected at the ballot-box."
In the general search for a new leader the atteiK
THE SAX FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, JAyUARY 5, 1899.
tion of the Democrats is at this juncture directed
mainly toward Gorman, who has a fairly good record
on tariff and finance questions, but who, nevertheless,
stood with his party in the Bryan campaign. His
colleagues in the Senate are strongly in his favor, and
he seems to have considerable support throughout
the country.
In a recent interview published in the New York
Herald Senator Faulkner of West Virginia, after de
claring his opposition to the renomination of Bryan,
said: "I have in my mind a man whom I regard as the
ablest leader the Democratic party can boast of to
day. I do not care to mention his name, but he is
now a member of the United States Senate." It is
significant that in no quarter does there seem to be
any doubt who was meant by the statement, and
e*'ery comment on it has been a discussion of the
availability of Gorman as a Presidential candidate.
The suggestion has not been received with uni
versal approval even among those who wish a new
leader. The Nashville American, which itself favors
an abandonment of the free trade fight for the pres
ent., strangely enough objects to Gorman on the
ground that while he "is able, a good organizer, a
man of brains, he is not in accord with the general
Democratic doctrine of tariff for revenue only.' 1
The more serious objections to the suggestion,
however, are those based upon Gorman's record 2S
the political boss of Maryland. His practices there
were so corrupt as to force the better element of his
own party to revolt against him. That record would
be fatal to him as a candidate for the Presidency.
However able a leader and organizer he may be, he
is best known to the country as a machine politician,
and he is, moreover, without magnetic or other popu
lar qualities. He is undoubtedly the first choice of his
Senatorial colleagues, but the day when a clique of
Senators could nominate a President has gone by.
The Gorman boom, therefore, is perhaps much larger
now than it will be in 1900.
ONE of the most important matters of legisla
tion this winter is .- ;:.e measure for defense
of California forests against fire. When one
reflects upon the range and variety of interests de
pendent on our forests, the subject assumes an im
| pressive magnitude. The lumbering interest may be
temporary here, as it has been in Michigan, Wiscon
sin and Minnesota, or it may be made a permanent
interest by treating timber as a crop which may be
; harvested and grown. .' re and horticulture
i and mining depend on I i ervation of moisture
Iby preserving the forest shelter of the mountain
| slopes and plateaus. Without irrigation our raisin
' product and most of our orange output will be lost.
The raisin must be grown in a naturally arid region,
where the heat and dryness add sugar to the grape
and dry it to a raisin in the open air. The fruitfulness
â¢ of the vine requires in such a situation ample irriga
i tion. The whoie crop of this State depends on irri
gation, and without it we would not grow en
raisins to pay for shipping to a market. The same is
true of the orange. It must have sun and dry air and
enough moisture at the root to make up for evapora
! tion from the foliage. Without this the fruit is small
and the rind thickens. Other important field nad
i forage crops require irrigation. The mountain for
i csts are the water reservoir of the State. Destroyed
i at the rate at which they are now being consumed by
fire, that reservoir will soon disappear; the soil will
\ wash off its rock foundation, and when rain
falls or snow thaws water will come down in irre
: sistible torrents instead of going into the shaded soil
to descend slowly into its natural channels and reach
the irrigator in such volume and at such season as is
, most useful too him.
The miner depends r.pon the same resource, for
water for his steam or electric power, and for running
stamp mills, washing gravel and in every essential
process of his industry.
Finally, the climate itself, prized possession of Cali
fornia, depends materially upon the preservation of
the forests.
It is to be hoped that the two universities will add
to their colleges a college of forestry. Cornell has
: done this, and has placed at the head of it Dr. Fer
now, long at the head of the forestry division of the
Department of Agriculture at Washington. The
State of New York has acquired 320,000 acres of for
est in the Adirondacks for the purposes of this col
lege of forestry.
California has a greater wealth and a greater stake
ie her forests than all the rest of the Union combined.
If the universities found schools of forestry their
classes will be sought by young men who have a
taste for this most interesting pursuit. I: the State
provide an organization for policing the forests in the
summer season it should be required that the students
of forestry in the universities shall have the preference
in that employment. Then will be opened a way to
save the forests, to aid tliâ¬ study required and to help
the many young men who energetically work their way
through the university. At Berkeley there is a stu
dents' woodyard, run by co-operation of students
who have to pay their way as they go. They buy
wood, saw and peddle it. From such material as that
the State could enlist it? forest poiice, help students
'-. and prevent the vast and destructive summer fires.
The young men would acquire valuable practical
knowledge in botany, dendrology and geology, lines
of learning necessary in scientific forestry, and we
would have a well-equipped army of trained foresters,
sufficient in number to oversee our whole forest area,
introduce economic methods into the timber harvest,
preserve the oncoming crop and preside usefully over
the great interests of the miner, the lumberman, the
horticulturist and the agriculturist.
By this plan the forest policy of the State would be
administered .without making it a part of a political
t machine. Its continuity of policy would be unbroken
and every dollar spent in it would bring a dollar's
worth of benefit with compound interest.
It would be well if the Legislature could have the
benefit of counsel with the authorities of the univer
sities on this subject. They both have botanical and
agricultural departments and professors who are very
competent to advise. The Miners' Association, State
Board of Trade and Sierra Club have already taken
some action in the premises, and doubtless have ideas
and information that would aid to a legislative con
clusion. The organizations of fruit-growers and
dealers and the lumber interests should join with
these and see to it that at least a beginning is made
during the present session.

VICTORIA CROSS- J. H.. Placerville,
Cal. The decoration called the Victoria
Cross was Instituted on the termination
nf the Crimean campaign of 1556. It may
be granted to a soldier of any rank and
for a single act of valor. The cross of the
Legion of Honor, as was felt during
the Crimean campaign, served a purpose
in the French army which was served
by none of the British decorations, and
it was in imitation of It that the Victoria
Cross was founded with the inscription
"For Valor," and which can be given
to none but those who have performed
in the presence of t|e enemy some signal
act of valor or devotion to country. It
is in the form of a Maltese cross of
bronze. In the center is the royal crown,
surmounted by a lion, and below on a
scroll the words "For Valor." The rib
hon is blue for the navy and red for the
army. On the clasp are two branches of
laurel, and from it the cross hangs, sup
ported hy the initial "V." ThÂ° decoration
iÂ«= accompanied by a pension of ten
j?oundÂ» sterling a year.
THANKSGIVING CHEER
AMONG SOLDIER BOYS
Down at Manila the Men From Minnesota
Feed the Favorite Sons of
California.
By Corporal F. A. Healy.
Special Correspondence of The Call.
MANILA, Dec. 3.â The coming of
transports is now of almost daily oc
currence, sometimes one sometimes two
{or three arriving in one week. They
all bring troops and the Eighth Army
Corps continues to grow in size, as well
as in grace and wisdom. Among other
bodies there have come to our hospita
ble shore the "terrible" Tennesseeans,
\\ho have gained for themselves such
fear-inspiring reputations for valorous
j deeds and desperate v.ork accomplished
during their campaign in San Fran
cisco. Their reputation has preceded
: them, and from all I hear I think it
) is safe to promise them the warmest
kind of a time as soon as they com
mence to "spread themselves." There
are a few "warm babies" in the regi
ments already here who are more than
' willing to constitute themselves a re
j ception committee and do all they can
j to demonstrate how kindly they feel
! toward the men from the South for the
; manner in which the latter requited
! Western hospitality.
Thanksgivin~ day proved a great
success. I did not see much of it my
self, as I was ordered to the hospital,
but from all accounts things were
made to go with a zip, boom, ah! Dur
ing the cooler hours r,f the day there
were athletic games ashore, boat races
afloat and a general come and have
something time everywhere. At about
3 o'clock in the afternoon our boys â or
at least those who were well enough to
do soâ became the guests at dinner of
the Minnesotans, They were enter
tained by their hosts company by corn-
In different restaurants of the
ry one had a first-class time,
and when the homeward journey cor
ned it would have been hard to de
termine by the devious trail left behind
which was the "field and staff" and
which the "rank and file." As for the
"line"â well that was so crooked that
a man had to turn around before he
could tell the way it led. The Minne
sotans are good fellows, from a good
place, and the celebration was the nat
ural outcome of the more than ordin
arily good feeling that exists between
their regiment and our own.
The honor of acknowledging this
honor done the Californians fell upon
Lieutenant Colonel Duboce, who is
n as Manila's "silver-tongued
A.iax." and gallantly did he acquit him
self of the pleasant duty.
The rick in hospital had as good a
time as their condition would permit.
Being an inmate of Ermita Hospital, I
dined there, partaking of an excellent
dinner provided by Dr. McCarthy. It
was spread under a tent, the g.:
Father McKinnon, and served on tables
loaded with delicacies from the hospita
ble Red Cross storehouse, the doors of
which were unlocked and thrown wide
open by J. O'C. Schlott.
It was a curious and rather pathetic
sight to look along the rows of tables
laden to the breaking point with every
; obtainable delicacy, and then glance
from the board to the faces lining it
wan, sunken faces, upon which the pal
' lor of sickness had overcome the bronze
: of the soldier; faces wistful and tired
: of expression, lightened by eyes which
would kindle with a momentary flash
of pleasure as they rested on the good
I things provided, only to grow dim
; again with the recollection of that
h<-me for the loss of which these same
: delicacies were offered in compensation.
However, the affair was a success,
', notwithstanding its environment.
Speeches were made by Dr. McCarthy,
Father McKinnon, Mr. Schlott and
others, who were, in turn, feebly cheer
ed by their guests. En passant, I
would like to remark a peculiar charac
teristic of the volunteer soldier. He
I cheers on all occasions and at all times,
i on the slightest provocation or without
I any provocation at all. He cheers
j when he hears a piece of good news;
j he cheers when the news is bad; he
i cheers at reveille; he cheers at retreat;
; he cheers at mess call, and he cheers
; to the very echo the speeches of his
\ superiors, which are. as a rule, on a
i par with the food. Tes, the American
â  volunteer soldier has a penchant for
; cheering, which is surpassed by noth
ing I know of. unless It be the passion
possessed by the volunteer officer' to
; make poor speeches at every appropri
! ate apportunity.
The rainy season is supposed *-> be
' over, but still it rains about three
fourths of the time, which, though un
pleasant as it makes traveling:
: and moving about out of doors
is really a blessing in disguise, as it
! makes parades (the abomination of the
i enlisted man), an impossiblity. Con
-1 eequemly. the boys spend most of their
time within the shelter of their bar
racks, discussing rumors which come
j floating down the line with a rapidity
I and diversity which would make one's
1 head swim. Who gives them birth,
whence they originate none can say.'
They always have been, they are. and.
as long as the army exists", they w'ili
; continue to be.
The latest one which comes to me as
I write is that we are soon to leave Ma
nila for Honolulu to take the place of
the New York regiment, which wil!
then be ordered here. Of course there
is not the slightest truth in this report,
but if it were so it would be an excel
lent arrangement. The New Yorkers
are very anxious to get here; the Cali
fornia boys are "crazy" to get ho:
as near there as possible. Transports
have to come and go. Why not make
the change and make every one h
We received the papers here re>
and noticed therein a statement by one
who. in diplomatic language, would be
characterized as "a. certain exalt-? i per
sonage." Said .personage affirmed, if
he is correctly quoted, that the boys of
the First California were anxious to
stay here as long as the Government
desired their services. The boys are
anxious to do nothing of the kind. They
want. almost to a man, to get home
just as soon as they can be taken there.
Their work has been accomplished and
they object to being used a.^ a stopping
stone to ambition, be that am
ever so worthy. Did all the i
spondents, whether professional or
otherwise, look through tie ranks once
in a while instead of over th-:-m they
would not only get a more correct idea
of how the men feel concerning vari
ous matters, but would discover much
food better fitted for reflection than for
digestion.
General King has arrived, hut as he
is ill with the ordinary climatic com
plaints Colonel Smith continues to
command the brigade and Lieutenant
Colonel Duboce the regiment. The
only addition to the existing conditions
of garrison life is the nightly concert
on the Lunetta. There is a bandstand
there and every evening a concert Is
given by one of the different regimental
bands. The Oalifornians play on
"Wednesday evenings.
Since my last letter -we have lost an
other man â Frank Temple, a private of
Company I, who died a day or two ago
from smallpox. Again death, following
the rule he has observed in almost
every case, picked from the best among

Rare Document Is Produced in the
Lee Divorce Case.
Hebecca Lee, who lives at 1015 Market
street, as plaintiff in an action for divorce ' .
recently instituted against her husband,
J. W. Lee, had her innings before Judge
Hunt yesterday. Mrs. Lee alleges that
for some time past her husband has been
in the habit of treating her in an inhu
man manner, and for these reasons she
asks that the marital knot be severed
and that she be given all the community
property in which her husband is inter
ested, together -with suitable alimony.
Lee denied her allegations of cruelty and
simply states that at the time he was
forced to slap his wife because she would I
remain away from home all day and on i
her return refuse to tell him where she !
had been.
Mrs. Lee was first called upon to tes
tify in her own behalf, and she Bet forth
many specific acts of cruelty of which
she was the victim. In support of her

THE GALLAGHER INQUEST.
The Jury Finds That the Fatal ->hot
Was Fired by Mike Flynn.
Coroner Hill held an Inquest yesterday
morning into the matter of the death of
James Gallagher, who was shot and
killed in Sullivan's saloon at 113 O'Farrell
street last Saturday night. All the wit
nesses were examined, but all who were
In the saloon at the time the fatal shot
was fired pretended that they did not
know who fired it. Delia Richardson told
the story she told the polire the night of
the tragedy, that she did not see the
bhooting. but that Gallagher told her
John, alias Mike. Flynn had done the
deed. Nothing new was elicited and the
jury rendered a verdict to the effect that
Flynn fired the shot, and charging him
with murder.
Flynn's case was called in Police Judge
Graham's court yesterday morning and
was postponed until next Monday.
Despondency Leads to Suicide.
James Terrell, a laboring man, was
found dead in his house, 456 Sixth street,
at about 4 o'clock yesterday afternoon.
An empty bottle labeled "carbolic arid"
was lying beside him, and it is presumed
that ho committed suicide. Terrell was
about 42 years of age an<l a native of Ire
land. He was a widower and leaves one
grown up son. Ytsterday afternoon he
attended the funeral of an old friend, and
this it is Buppoeed, induced the despond
ency which finally resulted in suicide.
The Champion "Fiend."
Frank Rlcardo, the young man who
swindled several grocery firms by obtain
ing supplies of champagne by represent
ing that he was a member of the Cosmoa
and Press rlubs. was yesterday held to
answer In $2000 bonds before the Superior
Court by Judge Conlan for obtaining
goods by' trick and device.
THE SAN FBAXCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, JANUARY 5, 1890.
THE SPARTAN ON HER WAY TO SEA.
After leaving Clallam Bay, and while the men were making fast the port anchor, Second Mate John Wielan
of the Spartan lost his balance and fell overboard. The steel hawser could neither be cut nor cast off, and the
attention of the crew of the tug could not be attracted by signaling, so the ship went on to sea ana Wielan had
to be left to his fate.

When the tup and her tow were well
out Into the Straits of Fuca the fatality
occurred. Second Mate John "VVielan and
some of the crew were on the forecastle
head making fast the port anchor. By
some mischance Wielan lost his footing
and fell overboard. It was a beautiful
moonlight night at the time and the sea
was as smooth as glass. The men could
soe the mate drifting astern and the cry
of "man overboard" was raised. The tug
was taking the Spartan through the wa
ter at the rate of eight knots an hour,
and the sailors tried in vain to throw the
towing line off. They could do nothing
with the steel hawser, however. Then
thfy got axes and tried to cut the rope,
but failed again. Lights were flashed at
the tug, horns tooted, danger signals giv
en, but the captain of thÂ» steamer neither
heard nor saw any of them, or if he did
paid no attention, and so the man strug
gling in the water was left to his fate.
The Spartan was towed to sea and cast
off. The tug returned to Port Townsend
with those aboard still in ignorance of
what had happened. Wielan was well
known on the coast, as he had been in
the Puget Sound trade for years.
The ship Wachusett arrived from the
Sound yesterday after a long and tem
pestuous voyage. To add to the horrors
of the trip she sprang a lf-ak, and for ten
Jays the men were at thÂ« pump:- more
s all the rim- 1 . It was on Christmas
â¢ .... when the ship was sixteen miles
southwest of Flattery, that the first gale
came up. It blew with hurricant- fnrce
from the southeast, and on Christ
mas day the Wachusett sprang a
leak and soon the water was making
headway at the rate of six inches an
hour. AH day long. Instead of enjoying
Christmas fare, the crow battled with
the storm and labored at the pumps. The
next day the gale died down a little, but
It soon sprang up acain, and for ten days
it was a continual nght for life. The ship
was well found throughout and thor
oughly stanch or she would never have
stood "the battering she got. As it is she
will have to go on the drydock and re
ceive a thorough overhauling before she
can go to sea again.
Vessels arriving one after the other
with tales of storm and disaster have
raised fears for the safety of the steam
schooners Ruth, Coquille River, Grace
Dollar and Fulton. They are all long
overdue, but probably were driven north
by the southeaster. The Ruth and Bonita
left Coos Bay in company and the Bonita
got in last Tuesday after a seventy-?ix
hour tussle with the storm. The Ruth
had the schooner Luella in tow, and the
chances are that both of them are hove
i he Luella is to be a steam schooner,
and is on her way here to have her en
gines put in.
The Coquille River was due here from
Tillamook and the Grace Dollar from
Grays Harbor last Monday, while the
Fulton left here for Coos Bay on Decem
ber 29. While the Coquille River and
Grace Dollar may have been hove to all
through the storm the chances are that
the Fulton has been driven past her des
tination and will have to fight her way
back.
Little Royal Mateo Beeman, the boy
whose mother braved the terrors of a
norther in order to bring him from the
Farallones to a doctor, died last Tuesday.
It will be remembered that the boat in
which the mother and her companions at
tempted the dangerous trip was nearly
swamped and all of them would have
been drowned had not the pilot boat
America gone to their assistance. Cap
t iin Jordan not only picked them up but
brought the party to the city. The best
medical care could do little Royal no
good, ho he passed away in his mother's
arms.
Vessels for the spring rush to th* Klon
dike are now being eagerly snuerht for,
and freights are likely to advance in con
sequence. Parties are now negotiating
for the charter of the Charles Nelson,
and if she is secured she will be placed in
the Seattle-Skaguay trade.

POST AND CORPS.
Joint Installation of Grand Army
Veterans and of the Ladies
Who Assist Them.
Last Tuesday niprht the social hall of
the Alcazar building was crowded with
la. lies and gentlemen, in addition to the
membership of James A. Garfield Post
No, Â«â¢Â». G. A. R.. and James A. Garfield
Women's Relief Corps, assembled to wit
ness the joint installation of the officers
of post and corps. The hall was taste
fully decorated with flowers, smilax, the
stars and stripes and military arms. On
the altar in the center was the Bible rest-
Ing on a saber that was used during the
war of the rebellion. After an overture
the ladies installed their officers, Laura
J. Robison, past department president, as
sisted by Josephine Robison, officiating
Following are the officers who took the
obligation and received instructions-
President, Dora IT. Wilkins; senior vice,
Sarah 11. Wilson; junior vice, Mary J
SothJers; secretary, Xeiiie A. Hendrick
pori; treasurer, Caroline Dibblee; chap
lain. Margaret J. Jones; conductor, Mary
V. Van Horn; guard. Margaret McCau
]<y; organist, Amanda yon Linderman.
Then followed a short programme, and
the installation of the officers of the post
by Department Commanler Sol Cabeh, as
sisted by Past Post Commander William
7rHan. installing officer of the day. The
new officers are: Commander, Edward S.
Salomon (past department commander);
senior vice commander, James Kiernan;
junior vice commander. Sig. L. Simon;
surgeon, Frank E. Kelly; chaplain, A.
Ennis; officer of the day^ Frank Elliott
Myers; adjutant, George a. Crall; quar
termaster, A. S. Isaacs; officer of the
guard, A. E. Cohn.
The ceremony was followed by the sing
fng of "The Star-ppangled Banner" by
Mr?. T. C. Masteller, the audience rising
and joining in the chorus, and then there
was dancing until midnight.
Fined for Contempt.
Tnited States Circuit Court Judge Mor
row yesterday concluded the contempt
proceedings in the case of the Sanitary
Reduction Works of San Francisco
against the California Reduction Com
pany, finding Behrend Joost, President of
the California Reduction Company, guilty
of contempt and fining him $250, with the
alternative of sixty days in jail, and Giu
seppe Figoni, an employe, -'$50, or thirty
days' imprisonment.
By a contract made with the Board of
Supervisors, the Sanitary Reduction
Works was given the monopoly of dispos
ing of all garbage within the city and
county of San Francisco. The defendant
was a competing company, and was there
fore enjoined- by the courts from trans
acting its business. The defendants vio
lated the injunction and were summoned
to appear for contempt.
PUGILISM THIS MONTH.
McConnell and Neil Readyâ Jeffries
and Kennedy Training.
Frank McConnell and Al Neil are train
ing like beavers for their 20-round bout
at Woodward's Pavilion on January 17.
The welter-weights have signed to light
at 148 pounds. McConnell realizes that to
lose this coming fight will mean to him
a permanent retirement from the ring,
and consequently he is taking the best
care of himself. Neil is by no means
easy game and will have several backers
who will back him heavily to whip Mc-
Connell on the night of the mill.
"Cocker " Tweedle and Tye of Oakland
should "put up" a rattling good exhibi
tion. Tweedie will doubtless be a favor
ite with the gamblers, yet Tye may give
him as warm a reception as he received
from Lawlor at the Olympic club. They
will contest fifteen rounds or more at 135
pounds. "Jockey" Maynard and Willy
Cole will box ten rounds- at 116 pounds.
All three contests should be interesting.
as the pugilists are well matched. The
entertainment will commence with the
"feathers." after which will come the
"lights," and then the "welters."
The big heavy-weight contest between
Jeffries and Kennedy will be held under
the auspice? the National Club on the
evening of the 27th inst. Jeffries is very
confident of whipping Kennedy easily, but
the Excelsior Club thinks differently.
The San Francisco Athletic Club will
entertain its members on the evening of
W. H. METSON, the New Park Commissioner.
January 12. The programme of fistic
events is: Young McDonald of the San
Francisco Athletic Club and Eddy Al
ford, late of New York, six rounds, for
the bantam-weight championship of this
State; A. (Jrunne-ld of the San Francis
cos and "Kid" Johnson of the Manhattan
Athletic Club, six rounds, at 123 pounds;
Jack Dean and Joe Long, six rounds; and
Â»lus Koster and J. J. Castro of the Wat
sonville Athletic Club, six rounds.
The club has received its incorporation
papers and is now established on a basis
with the following officers: President, D.
V. Denehy; vice president, John O^Keefe;
treasurer, A. Greggains; secretary. B. M.
Broad well; captain, Frank Scheuler; di
rectorsâWilliam Quinn, H. Gardner. J.
Grunfield, A. Dijeau, A. Minaker and E.
Turner.
TWO WEDDINGS
IN A WEEK
Members of the Hicks-
Judd Company Surprise
Their Friends.
The Hicks-Judd Company has added
two happy men to the list of benedicts
during the past week.
X. A. Judd, president of the company,
was married in Reno on Monday to Mrs.
E. A. Kelly, the couple having left here
last Saturday night.
The other wedding was that of Charles
K. Rosenberg, secretary and treasurer of
the company, and Miss Lucy 11. Munson,
bookkeeper of the concern, who were
married in this city Saturday evening.
The wedding took place at the home of
the bride's parents, 114 Broderick street.
Bishop Mor<>land officiating. Miss M.
Rosenberg, sister of the groom, acted as
bridesmaid and W. K. Munson, brother of
the bride, was best man. A limited num
ber of relatives and friends witnessed the
ceremony, and Sunday Mr. and Mrs.
Rosenberg left for Del Monte on their
honeymoon trip.
Mr. Rosenberg is well known in busings
circles, and has a large number of friends.
His bride has been in the employ of the
Hicks-Judd Company for a number of
years, and is highly esteemed by her em
ployers and associates.
FISHING FOR STEELHEADS.
Anglers Who Went to Russian River
Were Sadly Disappointed.
Deputy Fish Commissioners Cross and
Davis have just returned from Russian
River/where they arrested an Italian fish
erman for violating the fish law by using
a set net across the river. His
examination will be held in Santa Rosa
to-day. Last week the railroad people
broke open the bar across the river near
the mouth so that the steelhead salmon
could get into the waters above. It was
believed that the run of rish would be
large and the deputies were on hand to
prevent illegal fishing. The arrest was
made early in the morning, when thÂ«
accused thought lio one wan around.
Knowing that the bar would be opened
a large number of well known hook and
line fishers went up from the city, but.
they wore not rewarded for their trouble.
For some time after the break was made
tli" water was stagnant and but few fish
entered. Since then the rains have raised
the stream so that the water is too high
for good fishing.
Money in the Treasury.
The counting of the money in the vaults
of the City Treasurer was completed
shortly after 6 o'clock last night after sev
eral days' labor. It was found that the.
city has on hand $1,651,019 20, municipal
funds. There is also $300,000 on special
deposit awaiting the report of the State
Controller as to the amount to be re
tained here for school purposes. The for
ma) transfer of office from Christian Reis
to I. J. Truman has been made and the
office will be open for business at 9 o'clock
this morning. The office has been be
sieged for some days by people having
December warrants.
Advances made on furniture and pianoe. with
or without removal. J. Noonan, 1017-1023 Mission.
THE SAX FR Ay CISCO CALL, THURSDAY, JANUARY 5, 1899.
METSON TO HELP
RUN THE CITY'S
PLAYGROUND
Has Been Made a Park
Commissioner.
BUDD'S FAREWELL FAVORS
XIRKPATRICK AND HENSHAW
FOR YOSEMITK
Appointments That Find Favor in
the Eyes of the Friends
of All Three
Hen.
One of Governor Budd's last official acts
.was the appointment of two Park Com
missioners â one to beautify Golden Gate
Park of this city and the other to pre
serve nature at Yosemite. It has been
generally surmised for some time that
Budd has had the matter of these ap
pointments sifted down, but few who
were not on the Inner circle of his affairs
guessed the plums would fall where they
did. W. H. Metson received the appoint
ment for Golden Gate and Colonel John
C. Kirkpatrlck for Yosemite. The an-
nouncements were made to-day and
caused a little consternation among tho
friends of Kirkpatrk-k.
For some time the colonel has been con
sidered to be the favorite of the ex-Gov
ernor, and numerous friends put in a
word at every avenue that they might
furthor his chance. He was a member of
the Governor's staff, and not only did Re
publicans plead for his appointment, but
many leading and influential Democrats
Interceded in his behalf. Two points ad
vanced in his favor were that he is so
connected in San rancisco as to do the
park a great deal of good, and that for a
number of years he has manifested vast
interest in the public playground. The
kind words thus spoken impressed Budd
to such an extent that about one week
ago he persona^" informed Kirkpatrlck
that he should bestow the honor upon
him. This was assumed to be final, and
for a time some people breathed easy.
It happened, however, that this morn
ing at an early hour it was definitely
learned that Budd had decided to change
his mind, and this became a certainty
when, a couple of hours later, the Gov
ernor telegraphed to this city requesting
the colonel to release him from his pledge.
He gave as a reason that the Democratic
Senators and Assemblymen had protested
strongly against the appointment of a
Republican and demanded that a Demo
crat fill the bill. The Governor had made
his pledge in good faith, but the storm of
protest was too strong for him to bear.
When It was announced that Willam H.
WILL HENSHAW, New Yosemile Valley Commissioner,
Metson was Budd's choice the selection
suited everybody, and after the little
wave of surprise had shuffled Its way
along even the frienus of Kirkpatrick ex
pressed themselves as satisfied.
Metson is a leading attorney, being an
associate of the firm of Reddy, Campbell
& Metson. For six years he has been in
terested in the seed business and has
I made the subject of horticulture a life
long study. He ha 3 the largest acreage of
I sweetpeas on the coast. His beds of car-
I nations are superb, and are the result of
I his long experience and attention. He also
has the distinction of possessing the
largest patch of asparagus in the State of
California. His garden and vegetable
seeds have been distributed broadcast
over the land, and he Is considered an
expert on fruit trees.
Metson succeeds Irving M. Scott. He In
tends to improve the beauty spot of San
Francisco and will make every necessary
improvement within the limit of the park
funds.
Late yesterday afternoon the Governor
found a place for Colonel Kilpatrick, and
named him as the Commissioner for Yo
semite. The colonel knows every foot of
the ground like a book, and will manifest
the greatest Interest in affairs which per
tain to the State reservation. The last
but not least honor which the passing
Governor could bestow fell upon Will
Henshaw, a brother of Judge Henshaw,
who was also made a Yosemite Park
Commissioner.
TO PROTECT GAME AND FISH.
A Meeting of Sportsmen Will Be
Held at the Olympic Gun Club
on Monday.
A convention of sportsmen Interested In
the protection of same birds and animals
will be held in the rooms of the Olympic
Gun Club next Monday evening. It Is
expected that every gun club in the State
will be represented by one or more dele
gates. The object of the meeting is to
discuss the game question and the game
laws, and to submit to the Legislature
whatever changes and additions to the
present game laws the body in session
may deem advisable.
Owing to the rapid decrease of quail In
the past few years a short open season
will doubtless be recommended. The Eng
lish snipe will also find supporters at the
meeting, as It is proposed to include It
in the game law with other game birds.
Something may be done regarding the
limiting of game bags, but this idea will
not meet with general approval, as it
would be next to an impossibility for thÂ»
Fish and Game Commissioners to keep
an eye on the hunting coats of every
hunter who goes into the field or marsh
during the game shooting season.
Something should be done to protect the
wild ducks that rest on the bay from
fishermen who make a practice of netting
tho aquatic birds that dive for their food.
Thomas Casey, a well-known sports,
man. is slowly convalescing. His pet
English setter dog, "Dot," died last Sun
dny. and Thomas has been ill since then.
While out shooting Sunday Charles Os

WOULD BE NO BURDEN
UPON HIS FAMILY
JOSEPH WALKER ENDS HIS LIFE
OF TROUBLE.
Found Hanging in a Shed in the
Rear of His Resi
dence.
Sick, poverty-stricken, melancholy with
the sight of the pleading white face of
an overworked and hungry wife, Joseph
W. Walker, ship carpenter, put an end to
his 73 years of life by hanging himself
early yesterday morning from a shed in
the rear of his residence, 1552 Howard
street. The dead body was discovered at
6 o'clock In the morning by his wife and
his daughter Isabella. It was removed
to the Morgue. An inquest will be held
at 9 o'clock this morning by Coroner Hill.
On his person were found the following
notes, the first of which was in ink. It
runs as follows:
SAX FRANCISCO, December 2S, 1898.
To the Coroner of the City of San Francisco â
Pir: I don't think It is necessary to hold any
Inquest on me. The fact Is I was watching
on the tea wall to last month and I caught a
severe cold there. Through It I have a com
pialnt that I am afraid I shall not easily get
rid of. I am in much pain all the time. Three
weeks gene already and I am tired out. I
have written this so that no person shall think
that others have put me away. If I cannot
work I shall only be a burden on my family,
and that I don't Intend to be; so I think this
la best. J. W. WALKER.
In pencil nnd written at later dates
were the following notes:
I don't wish one dollar to be expended on mj
funeral more than is necessary, for It will dc
me no good, and my wife has no money. A
little laudanum did th>- w..rk.
January 4 la. m.â Three doses of laudanum
had no effect, bo I had to hang myself In the
cold yard. -T- W. WALKER.
