CHARLES A. BEELER
IS STILL AT LARGE
NO CLEW TO THE ABSCONDING
WELLS-FARGO CLERK.
It Was Rumored That He Had Sailed
for Mexico on the Ship
Reaper.
Charles A. Beeler, the "Wells-Fargo de
livery clerk who absconded from San An
tonio, Texas, with $50,000 belonging to the
Southern Pacific Company, notwithstand
ing every trap that has been laid by the
detectives to capture him, is still at large.
About six hours after the ship Reaper
left the dock yesterday morning informa
tion was given to the Wells-Fargo office
in this city that Beeler had taken pas
sage aboard her and was on his way to
Mexico. One of the employes of the com
pany was immediately detailed to hire a
tugboat and make an effort to overhaul
the vessel. Chief Lees assigned Officers
Ellis and Ferguson of the Harbor Police
to accompany him and bring Beeler back
to the city in case he was found on the
vessel.
After several hours of fruitless pursuU
the ship was finally sighted about twenty
miles south of the Farallon Islands.
Upon finally overtaking it the tug was
brought alongside and the officers were
allowed to go aboard her. Every inch of
the vessel was searched, but no trace of
the absconding clerk was found. Upon
being convinced that Beeler was not on
board, the agent of the company and the
two officers returned to the city.
Beeler had been employed by the Wells-
Fargo Company for nine years prior to
the date of the robbery. On the day the
money was stolen he was engaged in de
livering cash that had been intrusted to
his employers. Among the largest sums
that were given him for delivery was
$50,000, which he was instructed to carry
to the Southern Pacific Railroad office in
San Antonio. Several hours after the
money had been turned over to him his
horse and wagon, which he used for the
purpose of delivering the various money
orders, was found about five miles west
of San Antonio. Not the slightest trace of
the clerk could be found. It was reported
that he had been robbed and murdered,
but this was denied as he was seen alive
the day after the robbery. The presump
tion is that he was then making his way
toward Mexico, but knowing that he was
closely pursued he altered his course, se
lecting this city ;ls the best means of get
ting out of the country.
It was while acting under this theory
that the rumor was circulated that he had
taken passage on board the Reaper for
Mexico.
TO BE MUSTERED OUT.
Eighth California and Bateries B and
C, California Artillery.
The officers and men of the Eighth Cali
fornia Regiment will soon be simply plain
citizens. Major General Merriam will to
day issue orders that will cause the men
of the Eighth to doff the blue and put on
the garb of the citizen. Orders were re
ceived at department headquarters yes
terday for the immediate mustering out
of the Eighth Regiment and Batteries B
and C of the California Heavy Artillery.
Captain Pratt of the Third Artillery, who
mustered out the Seventh Regiment, will
I:i all probability muster out the Eighth
and the two batteries. One of the bat
teries is at Angel Island and the other is
at present at Fort Canby. Battery B is
doing garrison duty at the former station
and Battery C at the latter.
General Merriam said yesterday that he
could not state whether the companies of
the Eighth Kegiment now at Vancouver
Barracks and Benicla would be brought
to the Presidio for tho purpose of being
mustered out, but that in all probability
tiny would be ordered here. The Twen
tieth Regiment, United States Infantry,
which has been ordered to the Philip
pines, will arrive here about the last of
next week. General Merriam also stated
that Major General Shafter would arrive
here about next Tuesday to assume com
mand of the department and that he
would leave on the 15th inst. to resume hia
old command of the Department of Colo
rado. The Twentieth Regiment on its
arrival here will go into camp at the Pre
sidio, and will probably be hero for sev
eral weeks before leaving for the Philip
pines.
FIRE LADDIES ACTIVE.
Meeting of the Paid "Vets" and
Foregatherings in Prospect.
The fifth meeting of the Veteran Paid
P"!remen's Association of San Francisco
was held last evening in Justice Barry's
courtroom, when several new members
were enrolled. It was decided to, elect
permanent officers the first meeting held
after the banquet to be held next Satur
day (veiling. Several old fire laddies told
stirring incidents of the past. Richard
Cox, assistant chief, retired, George Ken
nard, Hugh J. Colvl" Fred Hensley, Cap
tain William Burns, Charles Bell and
Captain McDowell kept the meeting hot
about the hot battles to subdue hot flames
in days gone by under vie old chiefs,
Whitney, Ackerson and Scannell.
The Veteran Volunteer Firemen's Asso
ciation of California will hold a spirited
election for officers on Tuesday evening.
The annual meeting and banquet of the
Exempt Fin? Company will be to-night.
Postal Clerks for Iloilo.
The Postoffice Department has appoint
ed George H. Wheeler, William R. Jones
and Joseph A^ Fealy for duty in the
Philippines, an "increase in the Postoffice
force being deemed necessary by reason
of the contemplated occupation of Iloilo
by the United States. The city of Iloilo
contains a population of 303,000 and is the
next city to Manila in importance.
The new appointees will leave on the
next transport that sails from this port.
The Iliolo postofflce will be a branch of
the San Francisco Postoffice.

THE WORLD'S THEATERS.
Their Comparative Size, Earnings
and Income â Fires.
The greatest theater proprietor in the
â world is the Czar of Russia. No theat
rical manager pays so much money for
the privilege of owning places of
amusement. He owns no fewer than
seven big theaters in St. Petersburg
and Moscow. Next to the Czar the
largest theatrical patron in the world
is Emperor William, who assists two
theaters at Berlin and three in the
provinces, which together cost him
about Â£150,000 per annum.
These statements are made on the
authority of Edwin O. Sachs, who. with
a corps of assistants, has spent much
of the past eight years in collecting
data. He classifies the theaters of Eu
rope Into three groups â court theaters,
national and government theaters,
municipal theaters and subscription
theaters with or without subsidy. Of
these the private theater is. of course,
the only one conducted purely on a
business and speculative basis. Of
these, England is essentially the home,
for here there is as yet no form of
subsidy whatever.
Mr. Sachs has devoted considerable
time to securing diagrams of all the
principal theaters of the world, and
these diagrams give an excellent idea
of the comparative size of the latest
European places of amusement. With
in the National Opera House. Paris.
DOyly Carte's Theater and the Shakes
peare Memorial Theater could be cozily
stowe daway on the stage and audi
torium, and there would be ample room
for the Court Theater, Sloane Sauare.
In the grand staircase, and probably
the Duke of York's into the principal
foyer.
The theater maintaining the largest
permanent staff in Europe is the Vi
enna Opera House, where there are 756
persons in permanent employ, of whom
300 are performers. The Emperor of
Austria gives the two court theaters
a*t Vienna about Â£50,000 per annum.
The French Government contributes
the same amount to four Paris the
aters.
The most wonderful example of local
subsidizing of theaters is probably that
at Worms, in South Germany. At a
time when this was only a town of
23,000 inhabitants. Â£12,000 per annum
was raised by voluntary contributions,
the District Council subscribed Â£5000.
and the Municipal Savings Bank
loaned Â£7500 at a nominal rate of in
terest. The balance of the Â£25,000 was
made up at the bazaars, etc. The con
tributions thus amounted to over Â£1
per head.
On the subject of seating capacity
and cost of construction of the great
theaters of Europe, Mr. Sachs gives
some wonderful tables. The groat
Paris Opera House holds an audience
of 2166, and cost Â£1,500,000.
The new theater at Wolverhanvoton
holds exactly the same audience, and
cost Â£13,500. The Budapest Opera
House cost Â£266,000 for an audience of
1250, and the Vienna Hofburs: Theater
Â£550,000 for an audience of 1755.
Of recent London theaters. Her Mai
estv's Theater is put down at Â£60,000.
DOyly Carte's, now the Palace Variety,
is supposed to have cost the most
money, i. c., over Â£150,000.
Drury Lane is supposed to hold 2500.
Covent Garden 2300. and the Palace,
just referred to, was built to accommo
date 1562.
On the subject of theater fires. Mr.
Sachs records no fewer than 1100 catas
trophes between 1797 and 1897. The
statistics show that the outbreak gen
erally occurs on the stage; that nearly
half take place during- the night after
the performance, while only about a
seventh of the number nappen while
the audience is in the building.
January is the heaviest month fur
fires in the playhouse. Their number is
on the increase, for there were 360 in
this decade, as compared with 309 of
the previous decade, but the number
of theaters is, of course, rapidly grow
ing.
London heads the list of theater fires
with thirty-five in twenty-seven build
ings, but the loss of life has been nom
inal. New York has had forty-one fires;
Paris thirty-one.â London Mail.
A CONTRACT JOB.
How the Creek Was Quickly Turned
Into a Little Hollow Near By.
"I was only ten years old when I se
cured my first contract," said the well
known contractor, who was in a talka
tive mood.
"My father was living on a farm at
the time, and as winter drew nigh he
conceived the plan of turning the little
creek that ran through the farm into
a hollow near by, thus forming a pond
from which to cut ice during the win
ter.
"My father figured up the cost, and
finding that it amounted to more than
he had thought it would, was about to
give it up, when I looked the situation
over, and told him that I would take the
contract for $10. He laughed and told
me to go ahead, not dreaming for an
instant that I would have ambition
enough to begin on the work, as up to
that time I had not shown a liking for
hard labor.
"I called the boys of the neighborhood
together and placed the situation before
them. The nearest pond was a matter
of ten miles away, and even a boy with
a pair of brand-new skates did not
thrill at the prospect of walking that
far for a skate. I showed them how,
with a little work, we could have a
place to skate right at home. They fell
in with the idea, and we went to work
to carry out the plan.
"We were over a month working at
it; that is, the other boys were while I
stood around and bossed the job.
"I was proud of the work when It was
finished, and I think my father was,
too, although he couldn't see for the life
of him how I managed to secure so
much helf> for nothing.
"With the first heavy freeze there
was a rush for the pond by the bovg
that was only equaled by the rush away
from it when father discovered them
there and started after them with a
horsewhip.
"He said that he wasn't goin' to have
'a lot of fine ice split by a pack of
pesky boys skatin' on it.'
"I think the incident had a certain in
fluence upon my choice of a profession,
as it taught m^ that there was money
in contracting."â Detroit Free Press.

When the twins are born they are at
once taken from the mother, and. if no
one intercedes they are taken by the
feet, ar.it head,' and have their backs
broken across a native woman's knee,
In the me way as one would break a
stick. The bodies are then placed in
an earthenware receptacle and taken
t'j the bush, where they are devoured
by the flies, insects or animals. Some
times the little victims are put in these
receptacles alive, and are then eaten
alive in the same way. The mother bo
comes an outcast. If she does not at
once take her own life she has to flee to
the' bush. If Bhe ventures near the
town or village she must see that she
does not remain in the path when any
other native is coming. Her presence,
according to their superstition, would
defile the place for others. She must
not drink from the same spring, must
"not touch anything even belonging to
her- own relatives, and there is little
cause for wonder that she takes away
hfr life, which has become a living
death.

SOME SWEAR-OFF STORIES.
The Moral of Which Is That While
Many Swear Off, but Few Stick.
"I once swore off smoking," said the
clubman. "That was when 1 was young
and did not know myself. I swore off
with a friend, and the penalty was a
suit of clothes to cost $80. The condi
tion was that we were not to smoke in
town. When traveling or hunting or
fishing â out of town, In fact â we would
be allowed to smoke. The scheme
worked well for some time; then I be
gan to want to smoke. I battled with
desire for several weeks, each day of
the period becoming a harder trial than
the preceding. At last, when the long-
Ing became too great I went to see my
partner in misery to tell him that we
had better call the contract off, as I
could not stand it. He was not at home.
He had gone out of town a week before
ar.J would be absent another week.
Then I broke down. The thought of
the party of the second part smoking
himself blue in the face while I suffered
was unbearable. So, with the fine of
$80 staring me in the face, I bought a
and smoked it. It was worth $80,
I thought Hut T did not die for a lamb
only; I smoked a vast quantity of
cigars before he came home. Then 1
went to him and told him to go order
the suit, that I had broken the con
tract. He smiled quietly, as if to him
self, and then said: 'All right, old fel
low; but do not be disturbed about the
suit. I kept the contract just three
flays.' "'
Another man said: "There were two
men in this town who swore off, the
breaker Of the contract to pay th>>
other for a ten-dollar hat. Finally
one of them weakened, saying; to him
self ho did not mind giving >10 for a
smoke. While he meditated over the
matter, however, he thought out a
scheme to save himself on the hat. He
went to the hatter's and bought a ten
do! lar hat and had it charged to the
other fellow. Then he called on the
other fellow, and, pointing to the hat,
said: "See that hat, old boy? It is one
1 have Just had charged to you on that
swr-ar-off contract.' The other fellow
cried out: "How in the dickens did
you find out I had been smoking?'
r mind.' said the other. 'A little
bird told me.' "
"Well, 1 swore off," said another club
man, "and to make the matter memor
able 1 handed a dozen fine Havana
cigars to a friend, telling him to smoke
th-mi, as I had sworn off. I was like
the man who found the first day pretty
bad, the second horrible, and the third
no trouble at all, because he began
again to smoke. About the third day
my resolution died out, and then I
thought of the fine Havanas. I met my
friend. Where are those twelve cigars
I loaned you'. 1 ' I asked. "Here," he said,
1 knew you would want them again."
And he took one for deposit charges.
I smoked the rest."
'I swore "ff once." said another club
man, "and it was just as easy as any
thing you ever heard of. I did it by
logic. 1 found 1 was smoking one cigar
after another and never getting enough,
so I said to myself that if I had to de
â  myself at some time 1 might as
well begin with the first as with the
ith cigar. Thus I convinced my
self I would have no greater difficulty
than in refusing the steenth cigar, and
such proved to be the case. I had not
th" slightest longing for tobacco after
I had made up my mind that I hud
quit. I kept a box of cigars on the
mantel, for I said I mie-ht want to
smoke, and I did not intend to treat
mj self as one in whom 1 had no con
fidence.
"Now mark what happened. About a
month after I had stopped smoking I
began to have periodical indigestion.
followed by cramps, which came on at
a certain hour of the evening of each
day and grew worse and worse until
they were quite serious. I tried all
kinds of diet and even starved myself,
but without relief. At last, on the night
of a strikers' ball I was actually un
able to go out. although I had made a
great effort and succeeded in dressing
for the entertainment. I lay in bed
and groaned for several hours. The
next day was New Year's and of course,
there was a holiday dinner at my
house. Said I: 'I have dieted and
starved myself and it is doing no good.
I will eat one good square meal and
then die.' Bo I sat at the table and ate
everything in sight â made a regular
feast of it. Then, as I was booked for
the grave, or thought I was, I said I
would make a complete job of it and
smoke a cigar. I did not feel the want
of a cigar. The taste had not plagued
me in the slightest all that time. But I
smoked now just to go out of the world
in decent style, like a good diner
should. Then I smoked another cigar.
Having done my duty I patiently
awaited for the pains to begin. They
did not do so, nor have they at any
time since done so. I have not had
trouble from that day to this."â Mobile
Daily Register.
Calumet's Mixed Population.
The Western mining camp of three and
four decades ago has been immortalized
by Bret Harte and other lesser but capa
ble writers of mingled fact and fiction. i
There are to-day greater mining camps I
in the United States than ever before In '
its history, for the country now leads the
world in the production of mineral j
wealth. Rut the camps of to-day are lit
tle like their predecessors of the fifties I
and sixties. Mining has become more of a
science and less of a gamble; law, order !
and starched shirts have replaced the pic- i
turesque garb and more picturesque pro- !
fanity of the early days, and to-day tne i
miner is as apt to be a deacon In thÂ« i
Methodist church as a reckless mortal I
who divides his time between working on I
a grubstake when in hard luck and play-
Ing faro bank when flush with funds.
The greatest mining camp in the world is
Johannesburg, in far-away South Africa, !
where gold is mined at the rate of $5,000.- j
000 and $8,000,000 a month. The second larg
est is Butte. Mont., and closely press
ing it is Calumet, Mich. Both Butte and
Calumet are copper camps, and it Is a no- '
table fact that, with the sole exception i
of the Kimberly diamond mines, the most I
profitable mining properties of the entire I
world are those producing copper.
Ethnologlcallv. Calumet is a composite.
It is doubtful if there is another town in !
the country wher there is so small a pro- I
portion of American-born residents. |
There is the usual number of Chinese ;
laundrymen and a Chinese restaurateur,
Japanese curio dealers, Cornish miners j
from England, fully five thousand Finns i
from Russia, French-Canadians, Ger
mans, Irish. Swedes. Norwegians, Ital- !
tans, Austrlans, Slavs. Slovak?. Sloveni
ans, Ruthenlans, Dalmatians, Bohemians, I
Hungarians, Illyrians. Jews. Poles, Rus- I
sians, Hollanders. Danes. Belgians, Swiss, '
Syrians, Arabs and people whose nation
afties it is almost impossible to classify.
Ruffle*- It to say that at the present time -
there are no less than thirty-four differ
ent languages spoken in Calumet. The i
immigrants usually modify their cos- I
tumes in partial compliance with Ameri- i
can fashions, but many of them retain I
some distinguishing article of dress or j
headwear. The Italians cling to their full- j
fashioned trousers, cut upon the most
ridiculous bias, and the Finnish women
adhere to handkerchiefs of gaudy hue for I
head covering, while their husbands cling
to the home-made shoe-pacs, ungainly in
appearance, but much warmer and cheap
er than the footwear from Massachusetts
factories. Newcomers from the moun
tains of Southern Austria part with the I
cocks feathers in their hats reluctantly, !
and the Syrians exhibit their fondness for i
dazzling reds and yellows. One fact i
stands out very prominently amid this
Jumble of styles and colorsâ the Ameri
can-born children of these foreign deni
zens refuse to wear anything but gar
ments conforming to the American stand
ardard. The old folks can dress as they j
please, but the rising generation is Van- i
kee, through and through.â From a letter
to the Boston Transcript.

SENECA SWALM NO MORE
A Famous Figure in the Clara McDonald
Scandal Passes Away.
Â® /~*\ ENECA AUGUSTUS SWALM. who in his palmy days set a Â®
S pace in the Tenderloin that has never been equaled, and who Â®
Â® became notorious through his connection with the Clara Me- Â®
Â® \*-S Donald case and his subsequent imprisonment, Is dead. Not- Â®
Â® withstanding his trials and degradation he succeeded in keeping Â®
Â® his vow that he would not die in the County Hospital. He passed Â®
Â® away at the home of a friend, 1512 Larkin street, yesterday. Â®
About three months ago, at the time the accompanying portrait
was taken. Swalm, the one-time successful business man, with "money
to burn," had degenerated into a genteel and persistent saloon bum,
who obtained money from his friends to secure food and lodging and
invariably spent it all for whisky, walking the streets at night to save
the price of a cheap bed.
In the famous trial in which he was accused of stealing jewels
and securities from his intimate, Clara Belle McDonald, the former
wife of the now notorious â¢'Dick" McDonald of Pacific Bank fame, the
woman's testimony consigned Swalm to San ijuentin for seven years.
He nerved live and a half years, and upon his release wrote a book en
titled "He Shall Be Damned." It was a story of his life and a roast
for the McDonald family. The fabulous sum of $40,000 was offered
Swalm for the copyright, but he had the book dramatized and refused
to sell for U-ss than $100,000. He took a large number of subscriptions
for the book and collected a large amount of money In advance. How
ever, the publication never appeared, but Swalm took the funds and
what he hail when he left prison and went the same old hot pace as
long as he had a dollar left. He was the idol of the Tenderloin and
the best thing in town for the "cabbies," his hack bills averaging
$100 per month. He was regarded as the ablest life Insurance so
licitor in town and is said to have been the only man who ever suc
ceeded in writing a policy for the late "Jim" Fair.
The story of Swalm's life just prior to- the time he got Into trouble
is a very shady one and will probably never be written. Deceased
was about 47 years old. The immediate cause of death was paralysis.
He was a native of New York.

A NEW MEGAPHONE.
Orators lo Be Audible at a Distance
of Mijefe.
Provisional protection has just been
granted for an invention that is said to
carry the voice loudly and clearly for a
distance of miles.
It is claimed by the inventor that
the fitting of his apparatus in the
House of Commons, and connecting it
to Hyde Park, would allow of
thousands of people hearing in clear
tcmes every word spoken In the Parlia
mentary chamber.
This discovery of the power to mag
nify all sounds, both vocal and instru
mental, has been made by Horace L.
Short consulting mechanical engineer,
of New Maldon, Surrey. The inventor
has a varied and world-wide experi
ence, although only a young man. Edu
cated at the Risley Grammar Scho-ul,
Derby, he was afterward employed at
the Stanton Iron Works in the Mid
lands, at Messrs. Crompton & Co.'s,
electricians, and ultimately at the
Chatham dockyard. He resigned this
last position to go to Mexico as chief
engineer of the Compagnie Mint-rale de
Panuco. It was there that he brought
his experiments, with what is really a
phonographic voice trumpet of great
distance-covering capacity, to the
pitch of what he regards as practical
success.
A Dally Mail representative saw the
instrument working indoors, and had
an Interview with Mr. Short, who took
the apparatus to pieces in his presence.
The simplicity of the discovery is, per
haps, the most important part of the
Invention. Without divulging any de
tails which it is necessary to keep a se
cret until the patents now applied for
in different parts of the world have
been granted, it may be stated that the
apparatus consists of a phonograph
with special cylinders and bearing a
funnel-shaped mouthpiece.
Most persons have heard with tubes
in tht-ir ears the records of the phono
graph. The new instrument gives forth
the musical and vocal sounds in such
volumes as to be almost deafening in
an ordinary-sized room, and, ag Mr.
Short showed, he could increase this
volume at will.
"My instrument," he said, "will
work distinctly and with satisfaction
over two miles, and this 1 have actually
proved. It is only six months since I
returned to England, but the experi
ments made in that time convince me
that the voice can be conveyed over
treble that distance with ease. My first
Idea of its use was for ship captains
when in a heavy fog at sea, or for ships
passing each other at night at a dis
tance when their lights only were vis
ible. Now, however, I realize that
telephones can be made by the new
sound magnifier to amplify a speaker's
voice so that it will fill the largest hall.
This will allow of a political speaker or
professional vocalist entertaining three
or femr audiences seated in halls wide
apart at one and the same time.
"In time of war the admiral In com
mand will by means of my inven
tion be able to issue his own order? by
word of mouth to the different ships of
his squadron. Lighthouses may speak
to ships in dangerous positions, and the
vessels themselves communicate in the
same way with the coast by aid of this
Invention. Orderlies will no longer
risk their lives, except where abso
lute secrecy is required, in carrying
orders under fire from a commanding
general to the battalions of his army.
"Just as the power of the eye has
been increased, so that the distance be
tween the heavens and the earth has
been considerably decreased, so has
this discovery intensified the space over
which the human voice, and sound gen
erally, will travel."â London Daily
Mail.
DOWN EAST SAKDINES.
Not for Years Have Its Factories
Done So Well as Now.
There have been unusual evidences
of prosperity in Milbridge during the
Bummer, notwithstanding the fact that
it has been an off year for business all
over the country. The shipping has suf
fered to some extent on account of the
war, and there have been no vessels
built in this vicinity, but the sardine
fishing and granite industries have
flourished and a large amount of busi
ness has been done.
The sardine factories in Milbridge
have not done so well in years as they
hayÂ« this season. The herring have
been plenty ever since early spring, and
the factories have never been obliged
to stop work a single day for the want
of fish. The herring now are striking
off, and are not much caught in the
weirs, but plenty of them are found at
difforent points from Grand Menan to
Mount Desert Island, so that the fac
tories are assured of a good supply un
til they close up in December.
Thousands of cases of sardines have
been packed by the two factories here,
and as the price has been fairly good
during the summer the packers have
made money. As far as this season is
concerned the packers here are glad the
sardine trust was not formed, as they
s;iy that they have realized more from
their packs this season than they could
have got from the trust.
It is estimated that the sardine pack
this year will be double what it was
last season. The factories have kept
many people constantly employed, the
fishermen have found a ready sale for
their herring:, and everybody here has
got a taste of the prosperity. The em
ployes of the factories have received
fairly good waers and steady employ
ment, so that all have been able to lay
up Bomathittg for the winter. â Bangor
Whig and Courier.

DO NOT WIT
THE MARRIAGE
TIES BROKEN
New Trouble Over the
Stoddard Nuptials.
HIS WIFE'S PEOPLE ANGRY
THEY SAY HE MUST LIVE
WITH HIS BRIDE.
An Ante-Matrimonial Agreement to
Separate That Is Being Strained
to the Bursting
Point.
There Is more trouble brewing over the
Stoddard-'Wilson nuptials, solemnized so
suddenly and sensationally on December
13 la.st. The trouble again comes from
the your.g lady's side of the house, and
is due to the fact that Stoddard has re
fused to live with his young wife and is
about to commence proceedings to have
tlii' marriage annulled.
When Stoddard, yielding to pressure of
the most energetic character, agreed to
marry Irma AVilHon he did so with the
understanding that lie would give the
girl his name, but not his company; and
when fifteen minutes after the wedding
he left her and her mother it was "for
good," and he says ail parties under-
Btood it to be so.
Since his marriage Stoddard has re
ceived praise enuugh for being willing to
repair a wrong, but not much for the
wisdom of his willingness, the comments
coming chiefly from his father, Captain
Stoddard of Reno. The elder Stoddard is
not anxious to have the girl for his
daughter-in-law, and under his direction
young btoddard. is preparing a suit to
have the marriage annulled on the ground
of coercion, backed by the fact that he
has never lived with his wife.
When news of his intentions reached
Sacramento, whore, since the marriage,
Irma Wilson-Stoddard and her mother,
Mrs. Overly, have been staying, the girl
and her mother and her stepfather start
ed at once for here. They arrived Monday
evening.
The girl's people have had one interview
with the young husband, the substance
of which \v;iH an Inquiry on their part
as to what he was going to do, and a
reply on his that he was going to have
the marriage annulled according to agree
ment. They said it was not right, that
the girl's reputation was being Injured
by liis absence from her side, and he de
clared he had done nil they had demanded
of him. and for anything further they
could talk to his attorney, E. N. !>'>u
prey. There were some threats and high
words and then the girl's mother secured
the services of W. H. H. Hart, and the
fight is on.
"I have kept my part of the agree
ment," said Btoddard last night, "and I
do not intend to do any more. It was un
derstood at the time r married the girl
that I was never to live with her. and I
never have lived with her. There was no
reason why I should have married her in
the first place except that I was harried
by threats, and I thought to prevent scan
dal for both of us by consenting to the
ceremony.
"I made a mistake, but I do not intend
to make the further mistake of living
With the girl. One of the conditions was
that I should secure a separation, and
they have no right now to try to force me
to absolve them from their agreement.
They threaten to expose me, but every
thing that could be published about me
has been published, so what further is
said can do no more harm."
The Climate of Cape Colony.
The climate of the Cape Colony, and In
deed of South Africa, may be compared to
that of the south of Europe and the north
of Africa, with frnst and snow on the
highest lands and subtropical belts on the
lowest lying parts, as in Natal, Zululand
and Mashonaland. It may be roughly
sail that any part of South Africa is
better for those suffering from chest com
plaint than is the climate of our home
country at the most trying part of the
year. But those who come to South
Africa hoping to escape from this disease
should not, as a general rule, delay on the
CO;ist.

most people, but it is not by any means
best for those who are constitutionally
inclined to consumption. They must
leave the coast, n< m.-itur how many in
ducements there may be for them to stay,
and rlimb the tableland, which faces them
at whatever port they may land. At ir
regular distances from the sea we have
probably nowhere more than a hundred

LONDON LADIES' PETS.
Queer and Weird Creatures Which
Find Favor in England.
In the ordinary course of things
wolves are not regarded as desirable
household pets; but in the neighbor
hood of Regent's Park, appropriately
enough, there is one often to be seen
taking its walks abroad, securely muz
zled, and in the charge of either a lady
or a gentleman.
A Daily Mai! representative called
the attention of Albert E. Jamrach. the
naturalist, of St. George street East, to
the fact, and asked him whether keep
la* 1 wild animals as pets was coming
at all into vogue. His reply was that,
while wolves were not popular pets
among ladits, there were some very
weird creatures from the desert, or the
prairie, or the jungle that nowadays
found their way into a household.
Several instances were given. As a
rule, he said, ladies prefer small ani
mals as pets. A very popular pet is
the coati, which is sometimes
knuwn as an ant-eater, its home is In
South America, and it very quickly be
comes tame. It is, about the ?ize of a
badger, and can be safely kei>t in the
house, or it can be allowed to frolic
about the garden if there are not any
flowers in the way.
The suricat, which comes from South
Africa, very readliy submits to a quiet
domestic life. The late Frank Buck
land always kept two specimens in his
room. The suricat is a carniverous
animal, about as large as a ferret, and
is a great favorite with Zulus and
Kaffirs, who almost worship it.
Lately there has been a great demand
for the mongoose, a curious little ani
mal which is introduced in one of Rud
yard Kipling's famous jungle stories.
The mongoose, which no doubt owes its
popularity to Kipling, has rather a
fiery spirit, and is useful as a rat
catcher, but it can be remarkably tame,
and then it finds a warm place in the
hearts of lady admirers.
Up to a few years ago th<= kinkajou,
a native of South America, had a
prominent place in English homes, but
it ran away before the advance of the
railway engine, and is now running
wild in inaccessible parts of the great
southern continent. Formerly the kin
kajou, which has a very fine prehensile
tail, was seen in many houses but it
is now rarely seen. There is one In
the Zoological Gardens, and the keeper
proudly points it out to ladies, who
at once fall in love with it. Among
animals other than dogs which ladies
keep as pets are large squirrels, Aus
tralian opossums and kangaroos.
â¢ Birds as pÂ» ts have gone almost nut of
fashion, and there Is now scarcely any
demand for them. In the old days,
well-stocked aviaries were to be seen
in nearly every ladv'p house, but the
poor birds have been forsaken since the
advent of bicycles, and they are scarce
ly ever inquired for now.
At the present time the most popular j
birr! is the orange-flanked parrakeet,
which is delightfully tame, one of Its
principal recommendations beinsr that |
it willingly takes Its food while perched |
on a lady's finger.â London Daily Mail, j

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK, Jan. 4.â Stocks were reactionary
tn the forenoon, but forged strongly upward
In tin? afternoon, led by high priced specialties.
Including Brooklyn Transit, Sugar and Tobac
co. The opening was irregular with a tendency
both here and in London to take profits, and
prices reacted moderately after the initial
trading. Professional traders sought diligently
to reach stop loss orders. Many shares eased
oft considerably but a number of stocks show
ed marked resistance, notably Brooklyn Tran
sit, Pullman and Great Northern preferred. St.
Paul maintained a point advance on its remark
able statement for the fourth week in Decem
ber, showing an increase of nearly $252,000 in
gross earnings. A better tone developed in the
afternoon and continued until the close. Brook
lyn Transit made a total gain of S points on
Its showing of an increase of $664. 6;,; in its
gross earnings for the year. There were in
sistent rumors of a pending deal which insiders
would not discuss, but there were intima
tions that the control of surface and elevated
lines in Brooklyn might come into the hands
of the company. While there are large gains
In many cases and the closing was strong, the
advance in standard shares was generally
moderate. The rise In wheat stimulated inter
est in many of the Western railroad shares.
The advance of 6 points in American Tobacco
was unaccompanied by any new developments
In the property. There were large purchases
by interests identified with the company and
the movement was thought to be manipula
tion against the short interest, which has re
cently been created by the formation of rival
concerns. The Reading stocks were pressed
for sale in the early hours and declined sharp
ly. These shares failed to respond to the sub
sequent general show of strength.
The bond market lapsed into dullness to-day
but there was no abatement of the re<ent pro
nounced strength. Total sales, $3,^1
rnlt^.l States 2s, registered, declined 'i and
the 3s and new 4s, registered, H in the bid
price.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO, Jan. â The Liverpool wheat mar
ket was the chief influence here at the start.
Cables came Id lower for futures. Such ap
parent eagerness abroad to emphasize the weak
ness in this market caused the selling out of
a heavy line of long wheat here. May opened
a fraction lower with sellers at from 70% c
down to 70%e. So much was for sale that the
price was torced down to 70',-ic before intend
ing buyers could shout bids. The Atlantic
port clearances, however, began to have a
bullish effect when Philadelphia reported 350,
---000 bushels of wheat and flour shipped for ex
port. When other seaboard cities swelled the
total exports for twenty-four hours to over
1,100,000 bushels the feeling of speculators
found expression in a quick advance to 71 1 %c.
Reports of a cold wave from the north, which
it was claimed would cause severe damage to
the winter wheat, had a strengthening influ
ence. The market then became very bullish.
Shorts covered freely and there were heavy
buying accounts, which resulted In advancing
May to 71%e. That was above call price and
realizing on privileges caused a slight reac
tion near the end. May closing at 71%@71%c.
1 Wet weather, which was considered likely to
curtail receipts, strengthened corn. There was
also a good export demand. May closed %c
higher.
Oats ruled active and higher. The advance
was due to a good cash demand moderate re
ceipts, unfavorable weather and the advance
in other grains. May closed %c higher.
A disappointingly heavy increase in the
stocks of hog products for December depressed
provisions early. At the resulting decline,
however, commission houses had numerous
buying orders and packers who were free sell
ers turned buyers, the consequence being a
recovery of practically all the decline. May
pork lost 2%c, May lard advanced 2%c and
May ribs wire unchanged.

VISIBLE GRAIN SUPPLY.
NEW YORK, Jan. Special cable and tele
graphic dispatches to Bradstreefs indicate the
following changes In the available supply of
grain last Saturday, as compared with the
preceding Saturday:
Wheat, United States and Canada, east of
the Rockies, decrease, 2,123,000 bushels; Liver
pool Corn Trade News reports afloat for and in
Europe, increase. 2,600,000; total supply, in- ,
crease, 377,000 bushels.

Rockies, increase, 785,000 bushels.
Oats. United States and Canada, east of the
Rockies, decrease, 60,000 bushels.
The aggregate stocks of wheat at Portland,
Or., Tacoma and Seattle, Wash., increased
during the week 155.000 bushels.

BOSTON WOOL MARKET.
BOSTON, Jan. 4.â The American Cotton and
Wool Reporter to-morrow will say of the wool
trade: While the volume of transactions the
past week has fallen far below that for the
corresponding period last year, nevertheless
there is more than the usual amount of activity
for the opening days of the year. The market
has been influenced by the holiday season and
by the fact that everybody's attention has been
more or less devoted to closing up the books
for the year. The next event which everybody
is looking forward to is the opening of the
heavy-weight season, which will probably take j
place within the next two or three weeks.
There is a feeling of mild hopefulness on the
part of the wool trade as regards the charac
ter of the next good season.
The sales for the week in Boston amount to
3,079,000 pounds domestic and 717,000 pounds for
eign, making a total of 3,796,000. against a total
of 3,795,000 for the previous week, and a total
of 7,650,000 for the corresponding week last year.

Ginnt Powder declined to $5", subsequently re
covering. Contra Costa Water sold from $65
down to $62 50, but did better afterward.

small transactions.

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are Riven In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time. The
>â â â¢ â â¢ ond time column gives the second tide of
the dn.y, the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day. except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
Klven are additions to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign ( â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given Is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference If the mean of the lower low waters.

Office, located in the Merchants' Exchange. Is

I mariners, without regard to nationality and
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially Invited to visit the
j office, where complete sets of charts and sail-
Ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
information can always be obtained regarding
lights dangers to navigation and all matters
or interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building at the foot of Market street, Is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Naval
Observatory. Mare Island, Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
on time or Riving the error. If any. is published
In the morning papers the f^l 10 2' 111 * d .aÂ£

Many miners believe that the Interior
Alaska placer deposits are the result
of the same action and convulsion of
nature. For instance, a foot rule if
lajd on a map in a northwesterly and
southeasterly direction; will be found
tv cover the Klondike district, the Atlin

depend to- considerable extent upon the
map used. This is true, however erf
the latest official map issued by the
< General Land Office from the latest
records of the Coast, Geodetic and Geo
logical Survey. Following up this sin
gular theory the country between these
world-famous placer camps will be
prpspected in as straight a line as pos
sible where the. formation shows any
i-lgu of gold. It is confidently expected
by those who will follow this adventure
that pood ground will be i found.
Quart* mining has a bright future
.'ijriund 1 >a\vson, at least for the months
(if the year that the water runs. There
is scarcely any doubt about It. This
AVQuld -'change .' 'â â : Â« son's future from a

by., bats and Indians when the gravel
is worked out, to a permanent mining

quartz mines and stamp mills around
her, is the commercial and mining: cen
ter of Southeastern Alaska. One of the
besi looking and latept quartz locations
In the vicinity of Dawson w <s made by
George E. Pilz near Bonanza Creek.
The vein carries considerable iron, and
prospects about $15 in S"id. The ledge
is from six to eight feet wide and a
very true fissure in mica schist. Work
in the mines is going right along. Many
think that the amount of gold at the
clean up next spring will exceed the
total output of this year.

i SUICIDE OF MYRTLE
BROCEE, VARIETY ACTRESS
Shot Herself at Dawson Because
'â¢Nigger Jim" Fell in Love With
Her Sister.
VANCOUVER, B. C, Jan. 4.â Latest ar
rivals :'rom Dawson who reached this
city this < venlng by the steamer Rosalie
tell of the tragic death of the once popu-
lar concert hall performer of Victoria,
B. C, Myrtle Brocee. in Dawson City. On
the night of December 11, when sounds of
revelry floated up to her room from the
bar of the hotel at which she was stay
ing, the variety actress placed a revolver
to her temple and deliberately blew her
brains out.
"I am tired of life, dear Florence, and
see plainly we cannot travel together
much longer. You know well my reasons
for taking this step and will forgive me,
dearest. Please keep the truth from
mother." This pitiful note was found on
the rough deal table in the dead actress'
room and addressed to her sister. The
cause of the act was jealousy. Myrtle
and Florence Brocee, after a successful
season in Victoria. left three months ago
to seek fortune in the Klondike. It ap
pears that both were either in love with
or were after the thousands of the
wealthy mine owner, "Nigger Jim."
Florence was the favorite, and when this
became evident Myrtle became very mel
ancholy, and several times threaten*
suicide.
The sisters were Canadians, their home
being in Lindsay, Ont. They performed In
Chicago and San Francisco before going
to Victoria.

SAYS SUPPLIES
WERE ALL GOOD
Major Harrison Before
the Investigators.
EXCELLENT BEEF FURNISHED
ONLY TROUBLE WAS WITH THE
LOCAL KILLED CATTLE.
As an Expert Butcher the Witness
Declares That Chemicals Were
Not Used to Preserve
Meat for Soldiers.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WASHINGTON. Jan. 4.â Major Hop
kins, military aid to the Secretary of
"War, testified before the War Investi
gating Commission regarding sanitary
conditions at the camps at Tampa,
Camp Alger and Tamp Thomas. It was
the old question ( >f pinks, drainage and
camp police, and the testimony oÂ£ the
witoesa was little different from preced
ing witnesses. He had not approved
of the conditions at Camp Thomas ana
in accordance with his report an order
was issued on August 15 for its aban
donment. He knew of no adverse re
port ever having been made to the Sec
retary of War on Camp Alger. If thert
were any complaints they were merely
individual. He knew of no complaint
as to any camp that was not promptly
Investigated.
"What action was taken, if any?"
"Well, for one instance, I was ap
pointed with Dr. Reed to investigate
the condition of the hospitals. The
surgeon general told us to report daily
and he would act at once on our recom
mendations."
Major Harrison, Xinth Volunteer In
fantry, whose regiment â was raised
near Nev Orleans and Is now in service
at Santiago, was next called. Concern
ing commissary stores he said those in
the L'nited States were the best that
could be bought in the open markei.
'1 he refrigerated beef sent from this
country he : aid was excellent. He naa
had much experience with refrigerated
meat and he s.ud that while there was
some disposition on the part of the regi
mental cooks to cavil at the appear
ance of the meat, it was in reality quite
good. He explained that the sudden
change of temperature on removing the
meat from the refrigerator ships pro
duced what is known as a "beard," a
greenish growth that has to be scraped
off. When this was done the meat be
neath was perfectly good. It was is
sued to the men with excellent results
and benefited the sick materially. The
chief trouble came when the change
was made to hoof cattle. This beef
iiajor Harrison killed himself and per
sonally superintended its dressing and
cooling.
"But it would not cool in that cli
mate," he said very emphatically. -'The
meat never loat its animal heat, and
with its use our sickness increased 10
per cent. In a country where our dead
putrefied in six hours you will easily
understand that meat killed and ex
posed tii the atmospheric changes, with
coagulated blood and the attacks of
flies, will Bpoil much sooner. Our board
of survey had no work to do except
condemning and burning this local
killed beef."
' if the canned meat Major Harrison
spoke with equal emphasis, saying it
was fully as good as the refrigerator
beef and that none of it had to be con
demned.
General Beaver asked if a refrigerat
ing plant were established there
whether Cuban beef could be killed on
the ground and rendered fit for use by
the men. Witness said he thought this
would not be desirable, as the Cuban
rait:.', while line looking, furnished the
coarsest and most stringy beef he had
ever seen.
Witness said he was thoroughly I
familiar with the processes of refriger
ating and butchering meat and he was
willing to state positively that there
were no chemicals used in any of the
American beef that passed under his
observation. On the contrary, he said
that there was no chemical preparation
that could be used on the meat with
advantage to the packers and without
ruining their product before they could
get it to the army. He regarded it as
highly Improbable that anything of the
sort had been attempted.
Recurring to the hoof-killed beef at
Santiago, witness said the surgeons in
charge attributed the 10 per cent in
crease in intestinal sickness to the use
of the fresh killed beef. There was a
proportionate decrease in sickness when
the regiment returned to refrigerated
beef. Major Harrison paid he had no
complaint to make as to medical sup
plies or attendance. He considered the
whole supply system good.
EX-JUSTICE GARDNER
MARRIES A COUPLE
Officials of Marin County Will In
quire as to the Legal Rights of
the Former Magistrate.
RAX RAFAEL, Jan. 4.â Justice of the
Peace George Rodden was considerably
perturbed to-day on learning that ex-
Justice Gardner, who is also Town Re
corder, had married a couple from San
Francisco, and claimed that he was
legally empowered to do so. The names
of the two were Charles John Schwick
erath and Mrs. Lydia Dale Price, both
of 618 O'Farrell street.
Justice Rodden will contest the al
leged powers of Gardner in court. Judge
Angellotti will give no opinion.

COMBINATION
MEANS HIGH
WATER RATES
Must Pay Interest on
Two Plants.
"CALL'S" STORY ADMITTED
OFFICIALS TELL WHY THE DEAL
WAS MADE.
Explanations That Do Not Agree
With Statements and Actions
of the Past Four
Years.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Jan. 4.
The sensation of to-day was the fact
published i xclusively in this morning's
Call that tlk- notorious water schedule
- ted under such exciting circumstances
last March had been put Into force by
â¢â¢.. two watei companies and also thai
the two companies had positively com
bined and that competition was at an end.
These statements were verified to-day
by officials <>t" the two water companies,
who admitted that they had found it
ssary :<> demand the new rates, and
that an agreement had been reached,
n. >i that actual consolidation is a neces
sity.
The <'all is in a position to-day to em
ph.x.-iz. the statement made this morn
ing. Last Saturday there was a positive
agreement ratified between the two com
panies, i.i;. article of which is said to bo
that the two plants are to be placed on
one footing and under one management,
and that the officers of the Oakland
Water Company will soon be closed and
the Contra Costa Company will assume
control of the allied plants.
The purpose of this is obvious and will
be the means of yet further taking money
from the pockets of the water rate pay
ers. It means that Instead of the Council
having to fix the rates on the cost <>f
plant H will be required to
!:.\ them upon the lxisis of the
cost of the two plants, which, according
to the opinion of the companies' own ex
perts, Is about ten million dollars- six
millions for the Contra Costa Company
and tour millions for the Dingee plant.
Six per cent on this basis would mean
iver half a million dollars has to be
ted for interest in addition t" the
Ltlng expenses and cost of main
trnati' c.
Presideni IMngee of the Oakland t'om
d< clares In an Interview that th> re
was no alternative but to come to an
agreement and to charge what the ordi
nance allowed. He said to-day: "What
â  iture may develop I do hot know,
but as yet we have not actually consoli
dated. It . :. ;y essential that we
nave an in. reased revenue in ordi .
carry out our plans to supply Oakland
w;-;, water during the coming season
be a dry one.
X' Ither company has made money and
unless w t can g< t some return for our out
-1â¢ k 1 v ! 1 1 i may be without water and
tins will give thi city a very black eye.
'i n. dryness of the season mak<
â  to expend considerable money to
I - ;:l!ii L< :it water, and as a I
re must be additional revenue.
However, even under the new schedule we
â  â â  '. â  barging as much as th.- law al
ows by any means. We are merely pro
gainst a waste of wa
ur â¢ â â  bat hs, closets, lawns, â¢ I
"â¢Certainly, both companies will <-_harge
in. rate, and. as 1 hav<
companies have been forced to take the-e.
rhis statement of Mr. Dingee Bounds
when compared with the
statements of the Oakland Water Com
pany mad.' two years ago when competi
tion was ai Ita height At that tit:,-
Alvarado supply, on which the new <om
depends, was said to be sufficient to
supply a cay of three times the >{â /.â¢â  of
Oakland, and to be absolutely Independ
ent of rain, on which the <'ld company de
pends entirely. These statemi tits were
spread broadcast while the stocK of the
new company was being floated, but now
thing apparently has changed. The
president of the new company saye that
th.; citizens should be glad to contr
to any scheme that will Insure an ada
quate supply when prospects are bo
black.
: -tit situation is, from
a financial point <â¢! view, for the water
rate payers, they ar-- not yet being taxed
"aa. much ac the law allows, bj
b," according to Mr. Dlngee. As tne
rates â  er 500 per i â¢ nt ,-;ince
last January, and 31 pel cent since last
month, the taxpayer fe standing ag]
ring what km'! of a New dear's
joker has been sprung upon him by the
combined companies.
J. H. T. Watkinson is general manager
of the Contra Costa company. The bead
quarters of this company is in San Fran
and whenever Mr. Watkinson la
asked anything concerning the company's
business In the Oakland office, where he
presides, he invariably tells his caller
thiu everything is done in San Francisco,
and preserves silent c.
To-day he broke his rule, rind said: "It
Is true that the two companies realize
the necessity of additional revenue, and
to that end have reached an understand
ing. The Increase in rat< ais absolutely
Imperative, owing to the prospects of an
other dry season such as the last. The
rmes are not as high as provided in the
schedule adopted by the City Council.
They are only midway between the rates
of 1896 and 1898. Under the old rates we
have been barely paying expenses, and as
there is every indication of a dry season
coming, we have been put to extraordi
nary expense In order to maintain an ef
ficient service.
"Yes, it is true, as stated in The Call
this morning, that the Contra Costa com
pany has been obliged to open an im
mense pumping plant at Alvarado In an
ticipation of the dry season. The citi
zens for four years have obtained water
at nominal rates, and it is only right that
they should now be willing to stand a
slight Increase, inasmuch as the company
has suffered in the past."
It Is very amusing to Oaklanders to hear
the managers of the two rival water com
panies discussing the same question in
such an amicable manner and showing
how completely tlu-tr two minds think as
one. Four years ago the rivals were
rushing Into prim, denouncing each oth
er, circulating thousands of pamphlets
prepared by the Board of Health con
demning the old company's supply, call-
Ing -arh other thieves and resorting to
every known artifice to steal each other's
business. One of the main pipes of the
new company at Alvarado was blown up
by dynamite and the new company de
clared it was the work of some emissary
of its rival, while the old company sug
gested that it was done to create sympa
thy.
This spirit was kept up for two years
and the meanness to which the corpora
tion.-; descended is shown by the fact that
whenever a waterback burst in a con
sumer's bouse the opposition company to
the one supplying the house would at
once acquire the burst waterback and
exhibit it as a specimen of what might
be expected by using the other company's
water.
Then came the great revelation last
spring, when Oakland discovered that the
methods of the new uompany were not
any more along the lines of purity, re
form ami honesty than those accredited
to the old corporation. The scandals con
net. | ( with tli" Council were attributed
as much to one compnny as the other
and doubtless with justice, and all the
pi.as.vi! things said of the new company
were called back and the two corporations
stood on an equally unpopular footing in
tlie ' 'immunity.
Now the incident is closed and the tax
payers who welcomed competition find
tiiat they adopted a serpent, for both
companies being united will demand the
legal 6 per cent on every inflated dollar
they claim to have Invested.
To-day The Call published the receipts
of a water rate payer, showing that the
cost for his house had Increased from &)
cents per month last January to $2 .". for
this January. Mr. Watkinson says: "The
rates are not yet as high as provided in
the schedule adopted by the City Coun
cil."
Mr. Dingee says: "We are not charg
ing as much as" the law allows by any
means."
The taxpayer will speak at the election
to be held in March for a new city gov
ernment, and it is safe to predict that in
the next Council the names of Brqsna
han, Heltman, Earl. Watklnson, Wood
ward and Henntbcrry will not be promi
nent.
Trouble Over a Courtroom.
OAKLAND, Jan. 4.â There is trouble
over another temple of justice in Ala
meda County. James G. Quinn has suc
ceeded Fred C. Clift as Justice of the
Peace of Oakland Township, and when
the former appeared at the courtroom,
southwest corner of Eighth street and
Broadway, this morning prepared to take
possession his predei essor declined to
turn over to him the furniture and court
paraphernalia, unless he be paid there
for $2<k>. This price Quin;i regarded as
exorbitant, but ho was ai the mercy of
his predecessor, who had already paid an
other month's rent in advance. Quinn
has therefore rented a store jusf below
the. old courtroom, where he will deal out
justice until the friends of the past and
present Justices induce the former to re
linquish his key.
Burglars Still at Large.
OAKLAND. Jan. 4. No trace has yet
been found of the burglars who bound
L.o Chin, a Chlnes< merchant, this morn
ing and robbed his safe of $850. iiie
burglars had an easy task. They evi
dently knew thai Chin carried the key
of his strong box attached to his waist
by a cord, for after beating him they cut
the key from the cord and opened the
safe from which they took the money.
Sinclair Is Incompetent.
OAKLAND, Jan. 4.â The hearing of the
petition of Fred Sinclair to be appointed
guardian of his brother, Thompson W.
Sinclair, was concluded to-day, and Judge
Hall declared the latter Incompetent, and
as a compromise appointed Dave Sin
clair, another brother, as guardian, and
fixed the L-onds at $2000.

TAKE ANY EXCEPTION

CLIFT DISMISSED.
Attorney Chapman Brands Plaintiff's
Attacks as Purely Ma
licious.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call, i
908 Broadway. Jan. 4.

pose" defendant as Justice of the Peace,

When the case came up to-day Attor- j
ney >l. C. Chapman, on behalf of the de
fense, stated that it" there was any doubt -
In the court's mind as to whether or not
his -Honor had jurisdiction defendant
would waive that point, desiring tv pro-,
ceed with the hearing of the case, the
gist of which h< red a malicious'
attack and one in which the defense de
sired to put in its testimony.
Judge Ellsworth remarked from th"
â¢ he regarded it very strange
that Kinard had allowed the matter oi
charging malfeasance in office to lie until
within twenty days from the close of i
term befor-- proceeding.
"We have been busily engaged in h< :>r
ing this case," said Judge Ellsworth,
"and as yet Kinard has proven nothing.
The main object of the suit was to oust
defendant and the time for this has ex- \
pired."
Plaintiff Kinard here called his Honor's',
attention to the fact that a penalty of
$600 was involved. Judge Ellsworth, how
ever, remarked that he had proven noth
ing, and that the penalty was merely an
Incident. He therefore declined to hear
the matter further and struck the case .
off the docket.
Even to this action the defense ex
c< pted, stating that it would be prefera
ble to put in a defense.
His Honor then suggested that Kinard
might take an exception and get out a
writ of mandate compelling him to hear
the case further, but this Kinard declined
to do.

OVER=GENEROUS, THIS MAN
DIED ALMOST UNMOURNED
OAKLAND, Jan. 4.â The death of John N. Williams in Clare-mont a few
days ago recalls the story of his reduction from a man of wealth to
comparative poverty. About two years ago Williams fell ill, and he
thought he was dying. Summoning an attorney to his bedside, he
deeded to his grandson, C. D. Vincent, all nf his property, amounting to
54- pOQ f with the stipulation that during his declining years he was to re
ceive a certain stipend. Scion, however, he recovered, and the grandson ap
parently forgot to pay the agreed amount.
Believing himself wronged, AVilllams brought suit to set aside the
deeds, A hot legal battle ensued, and finally Vincent turned over to the
plaintiff about $8000 of the quarter of a million, and the case was dropped.
The amount returned, however, represented heavily mortgaged property.
This settlement did not patch up the family trouble, and yesterday, al
though Williams had many relatives, the cortege attending his funeral
consisted of two carriages. One held the minister and the other a daugh
ter, Mrs. Gray.
Another daughter lives in Sacramento, and is supposed to be the per
son who has been sending strange letters to Supervisor Talcott, in -which
she signed herself "God Almighty," filing up the body of the letters with the
word "whisky."
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THtTRSDAY, JANUARY 5, 1899.
IS CATCHING
DOGS TO PAY
UP HIS FINE
Farrell Given Just One
Day's Grace.
THE PENALTY FOR CRUELTY
POUNDMAN'S REPORT IS A REV
ELATION.
Casts Some Light on the Behavior for
Which His Deputy Was
Recently Con
victed. "â¢
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
90S Broadway, Jan. 4.
Pete Farrell, the Deputy Poundman,
1 who cruelly beat a small boy and was
convicted some time last fall, was In the
I Police Court to-day, but was not sen
tenced, although he was peremptorily
i served with notice that unless he be pre
pared to pay a $50 line I'or his brutality
j to-morrow morning he will be summarily
j punished. Farrell gave as an excuse to
day for not being ready with his coin that
his employer, the Poundmaster, was dig
-1 King ii up for him and would no doubt
pas it before night.
Farrell s employer, according to the re
' port of the pound for December, should
! have no difficulty In digging up $50. This
report shows that the pound has cost
Oakland a total of $is:< 50 for December,
and Councilman Girard demands an in
vestigation. This immense increase in the
fees paid the Poundman explains to a
great degree the reason for the plight in
which Pete Farrell now linds himself.
Farrell was convicted of beating a boy
after forcibly taking away his pet dog.
As the report shows that on an average
about fifteen dogs a day were caught and
killed, it is not surprising that some of
them were not the stray curs that the
pound officials are supposed to corral.
In addition to killing 318 dogs the Pound
master had collected fees for removing
51 dead dogs, ] dead sheep. 2 dead goats.
'S dead pits and .r> dead cats during the
month. From all these sources, as well as
in amounts collected from individuals who
redeemed their animals, and including $30
for the rent of the pound which the Coun
cil generously allowed for the first time
last November. Mr. Powers' bill reaches
the respectable figure of nearly $500.
To-morrow, when the deputy appears
before Judge Smith, the $T.(l will have to
be "ii hand, or Farrell will not be given
any further chance of catching dogs to
satisfy the demands of justice.
MRS. YON SCHMIDT IS
GRANTED A DIVORCE
SENSATIONAL CHARGES OF CRU
ELTY ARE MAINTAINED.
The Commodore Was Alleged to Have
Threatened to Cut Off Her
Head With a Sword.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Jan. 4.
Isabella yon Schmidt was granted a di
vorce fr'.m Commodore Edward A. yon
Schmidt on the ground of cruelty. She.
was also awarded custody of the five
minor children, fur tlv present at least,
and $75 monthly alimony fur the sup
port of herself and children.
The case was tried before. Superior
Judge W. K. Greene, and the courtroom
was thronged with friends of both parties
and witnesses from Alameda, where they
reside.
Mrs. yon Schmidt testified that on one
occasion her husband had taken a shot
at her in their Alameda home, and that
the bullet lodged in the door leading from
the dining-room to the hall, while the
sho< ter Btood In the kit' hen. On another
occasion he had threatened to cut her
head off with a sword.
Commodore yon Schmidt, the defendant,
also gave testimony which the court in
summing up the case severely criticized.
His iath<r. Colonel A. W. yon Schmidt,
was also called and narrated an episode
regarding a quarrel between his son and
th.- plaintiff, in which a flower pot was
crashed to smithereens. He had told
them both that they should be ashamed
of themselves. Witness regarded some of
Mrs. yon Schmidt's associatesâ mostly di
vorced women and theatrical peopleâ as
unlit and not proper companions, and in
speaking of the wordy wars that some
times were waged by husband ami wife,
he said that to his mind "the parties got
along about as well as most married peo
ple do." At times the wife was rough
with the children, but she had always
been what he considered a pretty good
mother to them. On several occasions
during the husband's absence at sea the
wife remained away in San Francisco all
night, and frequently came home very
late, say 11:30 o'clock. In the morning he
would comment on this and Mrs. yon
Schmidt would tell him that it was per
haps 9:30 when she had arrived home.
"But." continued witness, "I could hear
her come in and would get up and light a
match and look at my watch."
Dr. T. P. Tisdale, Captain A. V Lan
caster C. J- Lancaster, Alameda City
Trustee F. J. Fletter and Annie Mellon
were also called as witnesses, but their
testimony was devoid of any taint of
sensation. ,
The defendant was also questioned by
the court as to his financial condition.
He mated that he was receiving $250
monthly, minus $150, which he was re
(luired to pay monthly to Captain Dolllver,
his predecessor as harbor pilot, on a note
for $OF>O given for his goodwill: that aside
from this debt there were several others;
that he owned no realty, save three acres
of Alameda marsh land, worth about
$1000 and some Alaska mining stock.
Mrs yon Schmidt, plaintiff, next was
taken in hand by the court on the same
point, and testified that she was abso
lutely without mÂ»ans of support.
The case was submitted without argu
ment and in summing up the case Judge
Greene stated: "The plaintiff is entitled
to a decree. The weight of testimony is
in h>v favor. It is true that probably
all her testimony is not corroborated; but
it is corroborated in two very important
instances, which in itself is sufficient.
"The insinuations made on the moral
| character of the plaintiff when punctured
prove nothing. The disposition on the
part of defendant to pursue these insinu
ations shows malice. There was nothing
that called for the statement that she
was an illegitimate child, or that her
mother was an abandoned woman. This
was not necessary and did not meet the
issue. No doubt the defendant on reflec
tion would say now that he was perhaps
sorry for some things that he wished he
had not said. His calling one of his chil
dren names at the table showed him not
to be a discreet man. and his manner of
correcting a two year old child is not
commendable."
When the suit was filed, only about
three weeks ago it created no end of
surprise in Alameda society circles, for
both parties are popular and prominent,
and the fact that the wife prior to her
marriage had been a ballet dancer served
as a spicy morsel for the gossips.
Editor Weeks Will Betire.
ALAMEDA. Jan. 4.â is stated to-day
that George F. Weeks, editor of the Ala
meda Encinal, will retire from the active
management of the paper after Febru
ary 1. The gentleman has not found the
Alameda journalistic field as congenial a
one as he sought and for some months
has been endeavoring to lease his plant. j
He has been anxious to try his luck in '
Klondike, and it is said he will leave for
the gold fields early in the spring. The
Encinal will pass into the control of Ed
ward Xauman, who has been the business
manager for the past year. City Editor
Charles Whiting will retire, but the name
of his successor has not vet been made
public. The Encinal is the oldest paper
in Alameda County, and was founded by
the late F. K. Krauth.

GOVERNMENT TO PROTECT
STATE'S FRUIT INDUSTRY
UNIVERSITY REGENTS THINK IT
THE MOST FEASIBLE PLAN.
Chair of Forestry at BerkeleyâLegis
lature to Foster California's
Cattle Interests.
It was decided yesterday morning at a
conference of the regents of the univer-
Bity and representatives of various in
dustries at the Mark Hopkins Institute
of Art that the proper protection of our
fruit Industry was possible only if put
under the jurisdiction of the United
States Government.
This decision was brought about by
Professor J. Wickson. who suggested that
all efforts looking to the prevention of
the exportation of infected fruits to for
eign countries should bo national in char
acter. He drew his conclusion from the
rigorous inspection of the meat exports
from this country by the Department of
Agriculture, by which ' extraordinarily
good results have been obtained.
The conference is the result of the re
jection of a shipment of fruit by the Ger
man authorities. Professor Wickson read
a letter from the Consul at Frankfort, in
which the latter stated that the Presi
dent's met-sage had to some extent settled
the alarm created by the alleged infected
fruit for the reason that the so-called San
Jose scale is effectively destroyed by each
of the several methods of drying and ar
tilicial evaporation.
Professor Wickson also stated that the
endlin moth on apples rould be got rid of
by :i judicious spraying of paris green
and M per cent of the crop saved from the
insects depredations.
The discussion was brought to an end
When Wickson Bald that the bill intro
duced by Congressman Barlow in Con
gress providing for the regulation of the
Importation of nursery Muck and fruit
that may be utilized in interstate com
merce and for exportation will effectually
cope with the perplexing question. The
Department of Agriculture will be com
municated with after a conference of the
State Board of Horticulture, Board of
Trade, Manufacturers' and Producers
Association and the shippers of Califor
nia products, and will be asked to lend its
earnest co-operation for the passage of
the proposed law.
The committee of regents settled the
consideration of the dairy and cattle in
terests by requesting the Dairymen's As
sociation to have a bill passed by the
Legislature to enlarge the scope, of the
â work of the university nnd a proper ap
propriation for the establishment of a
practical dairy school. which wil be loca
ted at Menlo Park at the end of the year.
Professor Wickson recommended the ap
pointment of a traveling practical dairy
â¢ Xpert to give instruction and needed
help to the dairymen of the State. The
bill will also provide for a qualified vet
erinarian to inspect live stock.
In the afternoon the question of the
preservation of our forests was thorough
ly discussed and ably presented by Kili
ott McAllister of the Sierra Club. He
stated that Cornell University had estab
lished a school of forestry in the Adi
rondacks. and he requested that a sinfllar
branch be maintained in the University
of California for the teaching of a sub
ject which was of momentous import
to this State. Chairman Pbelps said that
the university should not take the initia
tive in the matter, and it was decided
that Mr. McAllister draft a bill providing
for a chair of forestry and its necessary
adjuncts, and that the Miners' Associa
tion, which Is vitally interested in the pro
tection of watersheds along the rivers,
take the matter in hand.
MOUNTED SWORD CONTEST.
Louis Tronchet and Ivan de Malchin
Will Meet to Decide the Cham
pionship.
L,ouis Tronehet, the instructor in fenc
ing at the Olympic Club, Is known to
thousands in this city as one of the most
expert swordsmen in the world. On Fri
day night. January 20, he will meet Ivan
de Malchin. Both men will at once go
into training for the great event which
is to decide the mounted sword champion
ship of the world. Professor Louis Tron
chet will train :it the San Francisco Rid
ing Club and Ivan de Malchin in the ring
Of the Golden Gate Riding School. Both
are magnificent swordsmen and depend
on skill and dexterity rather than on
force and chance strokes to score points.
Ivan de Malchin is the winner of no fewer
than twenty-six hard fought battles, and
the contest is sure to bring out a splen
did display of fencing skill and adroit
ness.
Lawlor Recommissioned.
Governor Budd has Issued a second
commission to William P. Lawlor, Judge
of Department 11 of the Superior Court.
Governor Budd's original commission ar
rived at a time when the legal situation
regarding the office now occupied by
Judge Lawlor was in an extremely tangled
condition. This tangle was caused by the
death of the late Rhodes Bordon and a
desire on the part of Judge Wallace to
claim the office, he having been fifth on
the list of candidates for Judge of the Su
perior Court. As the question regarding
the legality of the contention that Judge
Lawlor could not hold over under the
original commission was a knotty one
Governor Budd decided to be on the safe
side and in consequence a second commis
sion was issued.
