REVOLUTIONARY
SESSION OF THE
SCHOOL BOARD
Directors Evade an Injunction
and Rescind the Measures of
Their Predecessors.
The Department Restored to the Conditio]
It Was In Before the Last Week in
December â Principals Com
manded to Reclassify
Their Schools.
The Board of Education held last I
night a meeting that will be considered, '
In the history "f the School Depart
ment, revolutionary. Upon the recom
mendation of Superintendent of Schools :
R. H. Webster, the board, by a unani- i
xraous vote, rescinded all but one of the .
acts that were adopted on December |
2S, :)0 and 31 by its predecessor. The |
superintendent and members of the !
board admit that their action is unpre
cedented, but they declare that public 'i
policy and the welfare of the schools !
will justify their action even in the I
court. There is a grave question, even j
in the mind of Superintendent Web- |
ster, that the action taken last night j
infringes upon the decision of the Su- â 
preme Court in the Kate Kennedy case,
but if it does, the teachers that are in- j
Jured are placed upon the defensive !
and must take the necessary legal steps
to secure a remedy.
The board, by the adoption of its res
olutions last night, restored the School
Department to the condition in which
it was before the Ragan-Waller outfit
made the sweeping changes which
closed an inglorious administration |
with the year. In addition to this i
restoration the board made another !
last night and rescinded every order
made in December by its predecessor
and affecting the grade of schools, j
Whi'Â« the new board thus showed its |
supreme contempt for the old board, it I
took the opportunity to dismiss one of
its own employes to rea^noint one who
had served under the old board. This
action won a hearty tribute of applause |
from the gallery, for the opportunity
was one of mercy. A girl who is the j
sole support of a blind mother was
thrown out of employment by the j
change of administration. Vanderlynn :
Stow heard of it and asked his asso
ciates to dismiss their own employe i
and reappoint the first girl. This was
dene by a unanimous vote.
It was long af'er 9 o'clock before the j
board met last night in special session, i
As soon as the Directors were seated I
each was served with an injunction of
the Superior Court preventing the
board from deeradir.'r Miss L. A. Orr I
from the position of vice principal of ..
the Haight Intermediate Grammar
School. Miss Orr was promoted to this
post by one of the concluding acts of ;
the Old board. For a few minutes the i
Directors were st..Tgere<i last night.
They seemed, at the very beginning of
their campaign of reform, to be threat
ens! with the vengeance of the Superior j
Court for contempt. The weapon of j
Judge eleher was as dangerous to I
them as to Wallers and Ragans. There
w > nodding and bobbins of wise
heads, a harmonious blending of the
"club flush" and the "solid seven," and
out of it all came a clever ruse.
The board decided to rescind all of the
acts of its predecessor, within the j
period indicated, except that promoting
Miss < >rr to the position of vice prin
cipal of the Haight school. In this wav ,
the board avoided being in contempt
and won substantially all it sought.
Miss Orr will receive attention later.
At the meeting last ni~ht there was
nothing to indicate any but the most
complete harmony of the members!
There was a little balking when Grant
<'Â Cole. :i son of School Director Cole.:
was appointed to the position of assist- ;
ant storekeeper, but the disturbance
took the form of a simple negative from
three or four members. A little uneasi
ness was expressed 'hen Director Eells
beamed upon the Bcene in a dress suit.
but this was accounted for by the ex- â 
pl^nation that he had no other. He
was positively brilliant and may revo- j
lionize the dress of the board as well ,
hp its rules. It has been hinted that .
Mr. Eells intends to establish dress
suits as the official garb of the board.
After the shock of Mr. Eells' appear
ance had disappeared the members be- j
gan the a Tious business of the session.
As resolution after resolution was
adopted there was many an anxious
look from troubled faces in the gallery.
The school department is in a period â 
of revolution. The most important ac- !
tion taken last night, as already mdl- '
cated, was the adoption of the Super- ,
intendent's resolutions, rescinding the
last acts of the old board. These reso
lutions were unanimously adopted and
are as follows:
Whereas, The changing, transferring. !
assignment or election of principals, vice
principals special instructors or teachers
from one school, position or class to an
other in the middle of the school year de
moralizes the schools, classes and pupils '
affected by such changes, transferring. '
assignment or election; and whereas, the <
late Board of Education has made an '
alarming number of changes, transfers, :
assignments and elections of principals, !
vice-principals, teachers and special In- j
Ftructors In the month of December. l*>:^s.
froni one school or class or position to an
other In almost every school in th< de
partment under the control of the Board :
of Education; and whereas, such changes, j
transfers, assignments or elections mi- !
nosing additional burdens upon the tax
payers have been made without any good
or valid reason to serve merely the inter
ests of the directors and their favorites,
are in opposition to the best Interests of
the school children In particular and the, 1
School Department in general.
Ami whereas, the integrity of our public
School system, the best Interests of the tax
payers and the depleted condition of th^
school . fund imperatively demand that;
such changes, transfers, assignments or j
elections should be immediately set aside.
annulled and countermanded, in order to ;
neve our public school system from Irrep
arable injury; now, therefore, be it re
solved and ordered that principals of
grammar and primary schools will with- i
out delay r< classify with a minimum
number of fifty pupils enrolled in each
cIaJW, provided receiving classes shall
have S minimum enrollment of forty pu- i
plls; and be it further provided that In
the i'-classi(icatioi) principals may so re
classify that the average for their entire;
school shall be at least fifty pupils en- j
rolled in each class. And principals of :
evening schools will reelassify with a
minimum of twenty-five In each foreign
or ungraded or high school class, and av
erage t-hirty in attendance, provided that '
in the reclassilication principals may so j
reclas.sify that the average for their en-!
tire school ehall be fifty pupils in each ;
C sfesolved. That the principals in the j
classification of their schools consolidate;
the last appointed teachers in the depart- J
ment. All teachers who are hereby left
without classes shall report to the office
of the Superintendent.
Resolved, That each and every resolu
tion of the Board of Education adopted
at a meeting of the Board of Education
tiejd on the 2Sth day of December, by
which any principal, vice principal ,or
teacher of this department was appointed,
elected, promoted or transferred from one
position to another, or assigned to any
position, or to the unassigned list, or ap
pointed to a position, or promoted in
grade or degraded, be and the samf is
hereby revoked, and the position, stand
ing and salary of said teacher is hereby
declared to be not affected by said reso
lutions.
Resolved, however. That the resolution
electing L. A. Orr to the position of vice
principal of the Haight Intermediate
Grammar School is specially excepted
from rescission, in deference to the re
straining order from the Superior Court
of this city and county and Issued in her
suit against this board.
Resolved, That all positions and officers
which were created by any resolutions of
the Board of Education adopted at any
meeting of said board which was held on
December 28, 30 and 31, are hereby abol
ished, and all said resolutions are hereby
rescinded and annulled.
Resolved, That each and every resolu
tion of the Board of Education adopted at
a meeting of said board held on the 30th
day of December. 189S, by which any
principal, vice principal or teacher of this
department was elected, promoted or
transferred from one position to another
or appointed or assigned to a position or
to the unassigned list or promoted in
grade or degraded is hereby revoked, an
nulled and vacated, and the position,
standing or salary of said teacher is
hereby declared not to be affected by said
resolution.
Resolved, That each and, every resolu
tion, order or proceeding of the Board of
Education adopted during the month of
December, lS!>s. by reason of which any
school in this department was raised in
trade, be and the same is hereby revoked
and annulled. R. H. WEBSTER.
Superintendent Webster announced
that he had appointed Cecil W. Mark
as Deputy Superintendent of. Schools.
As the position is a statutory one in
the gift of the Superintendent the board
did not have to confirm it.
Three unusual communications were
then read. One was from E. Knowlton,
who, by the last board, was transferred
from his position as vice principal of
the Lincoln School to instructor of elo
cution in all the high schools. Mr.
Knowlton declared that he did not seek
the change and he respectfully asked
the board to rescind the action of its
predecessor.
The second communication was from
C. H. McCarthy, who is vice principal
of the Washington Grammar School,
and was also made principal of the
Spring Valley Evening School. Mr. Mc-
Carthy declared that he had not been
consulted and did not wish the honor.
The third communication was from R.
D. Faulkner, who is principal of the
Franklin School and was also elected a
principal of an evening school by the
old board and declined the honor. The
general resolution, nullifying all of the
acts of the old bos rd, which was subse
quently adopted, covered the cases, of
trie three school teachers.
Director Head reported that he had
ordered the dismissal of all the subor
dinate employes in the storeroom of the
department. This was done, the di
n-tor declared, in the interest r.-f econ
omy. The report was unanimously
adopted. Later in the session Head
moved that the Finance Committee be
on). -red to prepare a report for the
board at its next meeting on the con
dition of the finances of the depart
ment. The motion was adopted, its
purpose being to give the board a clear
conception of what must be dune to
keep within the legal appropriation,
notwithstanding the gross extrava
gance of the board that disappeared so
ingloriously a few days ago.
In the further interest of economy Di
rector Head moved the adoption of the.
following resolution:
Resolved, That the positions of princi
pal of the Mission Evening School, prin
cipal of the Richmond Evenine School
and principal of the Spring Valley Even
ing School now occupied by Madison Bab
cock be abolished.
There was some discussion on this
resolution and Director Head explained
that he urged its adoption because it
would cut off three salaries that were
not being earned. Babcock has been
acting as principal of these schools
simply to legalize his receipt of the sal
ary of each position.
Head wished also to have the board
reconsider the vote of its predecessor,
taken on the last days of last year!
As the board had already rescinded
every specified action taken by its pre
decessor it was deemed not necessary
to proceed as Head suggested.
As the meeting was about to adiourn
Director Stow arose and declared that
he wished to speak of a small matter.
He declared that one of the employes
of the old board, the stenographer. Miss
Cusack, was the sole supnort of a blind
mother. The director thought that it
would be an act of l.iercy to reinstate
her. The gallery applauded loudly at
this unexpected suggestion. It was
pointed out to the director that the suc
cessor of Miss Cusack would have to be
discharged. This was accordingly done
by a unanimous vote, and by another
unanimous vote Miss Cusack was elect
ed. The board then adjourned to meet
in regular session next Wednesday
night.

Ocean View Outrage.
The case of William Sullivan, the young
hoodlum, charged with an assault to mur
der upon Mrs. John A. Boyer at Ocean
View last Friday night, was called in
Judge Tread well's court yesterday and
continued. George Otang, the grocer
who went to Mrs. Boyer's assistance and
had two chairs broken over his head, was
able to be in court and swear out a war
rant against Sullivan on a similar
charge.

Dennery Against Ashe.
The election contest in which Leon Den
nery is trying to unseat R. Porter Ashe
from the State Legislature Is on trial be
fore Justices Kerrigan and Groezinger.
Testimony is being offered tending to
prove alleged Improper manipulation of
ballots. The cases of Jones against War
deM and James Brophy against Daniel
O'Brien are also being heard.
THE SAX FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, JANUARY 5, 1899,
The Late Frank J. French.
F. J. FRENCH
FOUND DEAD
AT THE LICK
Commits Suicide by
Inhaling Gas.
DISCOVERED BY A SERVANT
WELL-KNOWN ATTORNEY BE
COMES DESPONDENT.
11l Health and Financial Reverses
Combine to Render Life Un
bearable â He Leaves a
Family.
Made despondent by ill health and
financial reverses, Frank J. French, a
well known attorney and prominent Ma
son, committed suicide in the Lick House
Tuesday night by asphyxiation. Mr.
French entered the hotel at 3 o'clock
Tuesday afternoon and applied for a room.
He was assigned to No. 214 and took pos
session Immediately. He called for writ
ing material, which was supplied, but
nothing further was seen or heard of him
by the hotel people until his corpse was
found lying on the bed cold in death nine
teen hours later.
No letters or farewell messages of any
kind were found in the room, and if he
wrote any he must have mailed them
himself, but none of the attaches of the
hostelry saw him leave the building dur
ing the afternoon or night. At 10:30 yes
terday morning a chambermaid rapped
at his door, and receiving no response
turned the knob. The door was not
locked, and as it swung open an over
powering odor of gas caused the girl to
gas,P for breath. She gave the alarm and
the fact or the suicide was at once estab
lished. Mr. French had partially disrobed
and retired, after closing all the windows
and turning on the gas. He had evidently
been dead some hours, as the body was.
cold. The Coroner was at once notified
and the remains removed to the
Morgue. Attorney F. D. Brandon, with
whom the dead man was associated at
530 California street, was notified of the
tragedy by telephone and was preparing
to go to the hotel when Mr. French's son
Harold appeared in his office to Inquire
for his father. He said the latter had
telephoned to his wife in Berkeley Tues
day afternoon that he would not be home
for dinner, but as he sometimes remained
In the city over night no apprehension
"was felt. Mrs. French accompanied her
son to the city, but upon arriving on this
side of the bay they separated, the
mother going to the home of her sister
Mrs. Thompson. - She was not notified <>l
her husband's death until nearly noon.
Mr. French had resided in this city for
years, but removed to Berkeley on No
vember JO. They resided at the Colonial
until the Ist of December, and then went
to live at a boarding-house at 2247 Ban
croft avenue, Berkeley. They had planned
to return to San Francisco ti> live as soon
as they could secure a house, and it was
for the purpose of looking for a suitable
place that mother .and yon came to the
city yesterday.
Mrs. French is a daughter of the late
Superior Judge William Satterlee of this
city, and inherited considerable property
from her father, including the family resi
dence. Her sister, Mrs. Thompson, "is the
widow of the late Hon. Thomas 1,.
Thompson, who was Secretary of State
under Governor Stoneman. a member of
Congress, United States Minister to Brazil
and at one time editor of the Sonoma
Democrat. He committed suicide at Santa
Rosa in a sensational manner on the Ist
of February last by cutting his throat
with a bowie knife, while temporarily in
sane.
Mr. French's family consisted of his
wife and two sons, Frank and Harold
The former is married and resides at
Fruitvale. He is a railway mail clerk on
the Southern Pacific between this city
and Los Angeles, and was on the run
when the tragedy occurred.
Uarold is only I'm years old. and was a
student at the University of California
until he went to the Klondike a year ago
and returned early in November. ' He was
preparing to leave again for Alaska some
time during the present month.
Deceased was born In Fayette, Kenne
bec County, Me., nearly sixty years ago.
and came to California, in 1860, locating
in Siskiyou County, where he served as
Deputy County Clerk. He afterward re
moved to Tehama County, where he
served successively as County Clerk, Au
ditor and Recorder. He came to San
Francisco in 1868, and was soon after
ward admitted to the bar. He served one
term as a member of the Board of Educa
tion, and was regarded by all who knew
him as an upright, conscientious man.
He was a member of Occidental Lodge of
Masons and past grand commander of
Golden Gate Commandery. Knights Tem
plar.
The inqur-st will be held at the Morgue
at 10 o'clork this morning. The funeral,
which will be under the auspices of Occi
dental Lodge of Masons, will be held
from Masonic Temple Friday at 12:30 p. m.
The interment will be at Mountain View,
Oakland.

THE CITY HALL IS
FINALLY COMPLETED
FORMAL TRANSFER OF THE
HUGE PILE.
It Has Been in Course of Construction
Twenty-Seven Years and Cost
$5,723,794 18.
The City Hall has at last been turned
over to the Board of Supervisors as com
plete. The cornerstone of the big build
ing was laid February 22, 1872, and the
building was practically completed last
year. its total cost was $5,723,794 18, and
the total receipts of the commission from
all sources were $.~>,724.03S 98. Upon retir
ing from office they left $51 30 in the fund.
It is said a new appropriation could have
been secured from the Legislature, but
the commission determined not to ask for
one.
There were no ceremonies of any char
acter when the historic pile was formally
placed in charge of the Supervisors. A
formal report was submitted, giving in
detail a history of the hall from the time
the enabling act was passed in 1870 up
to the present time. The building has
been occupied as its different sections
were ready for occupancy. The courts
and municipal offices were the first to
desert the old hall, the last occupants of
that building being the various branches
of the- Police Department.
Since IkT: v . the City Hail Commission has
consisted of thi- Mayor, Auditor ami City
and County Attorney. The Commission
ers were allowed a salary of Jim) per
month, but in recent years they have not
collected tin- fee.
AN ATTORNEY'S HEROIC ACT.
Thomas Keough Saves a Woman
From Being Burned to Death.
Attorney Thomas Keough of the firm of
Reddy, Campbell & Metson is carrying
his left arm in a sling and it is owing to
his presence of mind and bravery in sav
ing a woman from being burned to death.
Keough was on a Jones street car on
Tuesday night, and when the car was
ncaring Geary street he saw a woman
at the open window of a house calling
for help. He jumped off the car and
rushed Into the house. The woman's
dress was on lire, the result of a lamp ex
ploding, and she made a rush for the
door as Keough entered. He grabbed
hold of her with his right hand and tore
the burning clothing off her with his left,
succeeding in saving her life. She was
slightly burned about the face and arms.
Keouph's left hand was badly burned in
tearing off the burning clothing.
After his heroic act Mr. Keough hur
ried out of the house to a physician to
have his hand dressed without taking the
trouble to ask the name of the. woman
whose life he had saved.

A Missing Klondiker.
Captain Hohen has received a letter
from Mrs. Birdie Williams of Hume, Mo.,
asking for information about her broth- :
er, Dan Coots. He left Dawson City
about September 1 and at St. Michaels
took passage on the steamer Portland.
He reached this city but then has
not been heard of. He was on his way I
home to support his sister, a widow, and j
her little daughter.

CHRISTIANSEN'S
MACHINE MUCH
TOO COMPLEX
Trying the Mechanical
Voter.
OPINION OF AN EXPERT
WOULD DESTROY THE PRIVACY
OF THE BALLOT.
Defects Which Would Seriously In
terfere With the Expression
of the People's
Voice.
Inventor Christiansen gave a "voting
afternoon" yesterday which was more or
less of a success. Christiansen received
and his voting machine entertained. He
asked every one to vote and to criticize
âan invitation promptly accepted by the
few who were there, and the result was
not entirely satisfactoryâ to the machine
and its owner.
The Christiansen machine was the one
that raised such a storm, partly over its
political success and partly over Its me
chanical defects. It was the one through
which the suffragists of the State were
to record their choice whether they liked
It or not, and despite a demand by the
people for an efficient machine, and when
its rods tangled and its mechanism
snapped and its dials refused to record
the inventor had some difficulty in ex
plaining that the mechanical tally clerk
had yet a few trifling alterations to un
dergo.
Among those who inspected the clock
work politician was an expert machinist,
sent by The Call, and his report is any
thing but cheering. The vote-maker is
built on good principlesâ the mechanical
recording of the people's choiceâ but like
some other party workers it is not above
reproach. There are a lot of rods to be
twisted and sometimes they work and
sometimes they don't. A voter has to
pull a rod and give it a quarter turn and
swear at it. There is a whirr and a rat
tle, the rod whacks a number on a small
dial and a vote Is recorded for one of the
candidates, presumably the one intended.
It is much more complicated than the
average tally clerk, but it does not drink
nor smoke, nor does it draw pay after
being adopted by the legislature.
In giving his opinion of the machine
The Call's expert said:
"I am afraid Mr. Christiansen's machine
needs to be simplified considerably. The
mechanical principles upon which the ma
chine Is based arc perfectly sound, but a
voter would have to be extremely careful
in using it lest he should fail to cast his
vote. In my mind, the machine does not
meet the requirements of a voting ma
chine. It is altogether too complex. In
its present state 1 do not believe there is
one ordinary Individual in a hundred who
could use it withoul making a mistake,
and the election officials could hardly
instruct the voters in the manipulation
of the vote-casting rods. There is also a
very grave danger of the mechanism of
the machine getting out of gear on elec
tion day.
"One feature of the invention that is open
to criticism is that it is possible to easily
disarrange the machine after the voter
has cast his vote. Also, the figures on the
indicating rods are too small, a defect
which would render the counting of the
votes a rather complicated job. A voter
in lasting his vote lifts the voting rod
opposite the name or number of the candi
date he wishes to vote for, anri. the rod
is then held in suspension. The act of
giving the rod a quarter turn records the
vote on the indicating screw.
"Should the voter be careless and forget
to give the rod a full quarter turn, that
particular vote would not register. This
Is a serious defect, and it would have to
be obviated by. some mechanical contriv
ance.
"The adoption of such a machine would
bid fair to destroy the privacy of the
ballot."
MISS ATHERTON
IS MARRIED
The Novelist's Daughter
United to Albert Rus-
sell.
Interesting, though unusually quiet,
on account of a recent death in the
family, was the wedding of Miss Muriel
Atherton and Albert Russell, celebrated
last evening at the residence of the
bride's aunt, Mrs. Edward Eyre, 2209
Buchanan street.
When the engagement of the popular
young couple was first announced it was
intended that their wedding should be one
of the events of the social season. The
death of the bride's grandmother, Mrs.
Uhlhorn, was the occasion of altering all
plans, and the nuptial ceremony was sim
ple and quiet, with only the immediate
relatives in attendance.
The fair young bride, who is noted for
her beauty as well as her many charming
traits of character, is the daughter of the
well-known novelist, Mrs. Gertrude Ath
erton. Mrs. Atherton, who makes her
home in London, is at present visiting in
Washington, I>. C. Mr. Russell, the happy
man. is well known in social and business
circles, and holds a responsible position
in the Western Sugar Refinery Company.
Rev. Bather Ramm of St. Mary's united
the happy young couple. Miss Frances
Moore attended the bride as maid of
honor and Edward H. Sheldon officiated
in the capacity of best man.
The bride looked sweet and charming
in a gown of white satin. There is to bÂ»Â»
no honeymoon, and Mr. and Mrs. Russell
will at once take up their residence in
this city.
The Equitable Life's Statement.
The preliminary statement of the Equit
able Life Assurance Society, which we
publish in another column, shows that
the assets of the society have now passed
the $260,000,000 mark, while the surplus has
reached the enormous amount of $55,000,
--000. During the past year this society
issued new assurance to the amount of
$170,000,000, and now has outstanding as
surance to the amount of almost a billion
dollars, the actual figures given in the
statement being $980,000,000.
The successful record (if this society
during 1898, as shown by its preliminary
statement, should be a cause of great
gratification to the policy holders of the
society, and. as a matter of fact, to all
who believe in life assurance, and that
includes the great majority of people.
Not Damaged by Fire.
The underwriters have made an exam
ination of the premises visited by fire on
Stevenson and First streets last Sunday
and found that the first estimates of the
damage done were in excess of the actual
hiss. For example, it was supposed that
the American Carbonic Acid Gas Com
pany's plant and machinery, at 19 Steven
son street, was an entire loss. Such was
not the case. The company's plant is
next door to where the tire- was burning
and escaped without any damage. Had
the blaze reached these premises it would
have done considerable damage, but no
loss was sustained.

OFFICIAL CHANGES
IN POLICE COUHTS
NEW PROSECUTING ATTORNEYS
AND CLERKS TAKE OFFICE.
Complimentary Remarks Passed on
Some of the Incumbents and
Their Predecessors.
The Police Court officials appointed by
the Board of Supervisors assumed their
respective duties yesterday morning, and
the old hands gave way to the new.
In Judge Slogan's court Prosecuting At
torney Spinetti introduced his successor,
ex-Judge Joachimsen, in a few well
chosen and complimentary words. Judge
Alogan added his quota of praise and the
new prosecuting attorney returned
thanks. Sam Kohlman, clerk of the
court, and "Warrant Clerk Mann gave way
to Fred Conway and Julius Pundt re
spectively, but nut before Judge Mogan
had paid Kohlman a well-deserved com
pliment.
Ex-Judge Low assumed the duties of
prosecuting attorney in Judge Conlan'a
court without tlie lurmality of an intro
duction, as his predecessor was not pres
ent to give him the glad hand. Harry
Kivers haiwled over the position of clerk
of the court to his successor, John P.
Rice, and .Warrant Clerk Hogan, who hag
many friends, retired in favor ot R. E. H.
Lyons.
Q. A. Wentworth was introduced as
prosecuting attorney in Judge Grahams
court by Assistant Prosecuting Attorney
Drury, "who said he had known him for
years and was satisfied he would dis
charge the duties satisfactorily. Went
worth made an appropriate reply. War
rant Clerk Drury; who has made a host
of friends by his genial and courteous
I manners, and'able discharge of his duties,
I retired in favor of W. J. Attridge, and
Clerk of the Court Hayes in favor of
Thomas Kelly.
Before introducing his successor, Prose-
I outing. Attorney Reynolds of No. 4 court
I took occasion to thank the court, the
court officials and the press for courtesies
extended to him for the past two years.
L G. Carpenter, his successor, remarked
that he had always not alon^ r well with
the officials in the department, and he
had no doubt that amicable relations
would exist in the future. Warrant Clerk
McGovem retired in favor of Bert Wren
i and Clerk of the Court Haskins in favor
iof James McQuade. Judge Treadwell was
i sick, and Justice of the Peace Barry occu
pied the bench.
SAY RAFFAEL IS TRUE.
Friends Claim That the Singer Has
Not Deserted His Wife.
In spite of the rumors floating around
town concerning Jack Raffael am! his al
leged desertion of his wjfe and child, the
friends of the Jackson family strenuously
deny the story.
They claim that Mr. Raffael's sojourn in
the East is compelled by the exigencies
or his profession, and that he is at pres
ent in Philadelphia, a member of the
Stock well Opera Company. Mrs. Raffaol,
it is asserted, la in constani communica
tion with him and proposes to join him just
as soon as her condition yriU allow her to
bear the fatigue of an Eastern journey.
A Soldier's Mascot.
Albert Lang, a boy 14 years of age, was
taken to the City Prison yesterday by
Officer Coleman of the Pacific Coast So
ciety for the Prevention of Cruelty to
j Children. The boy's parents have
i been divorced and he has been running
wild. Two months ago be wds adopted
by Company B. Eighth California Regi
ment, at the Presidio, as its mascot and
he was supplied with a uniform. He will
be sent to a family in Petaluma. who will
adopt him. He is a bright boy.
